The Alleged Shirk of Adam and Eve: Objection and Answer
Refutes the claim that Adam and Eve committed shirk by contextual interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological responses to the apparent implication of shirk by Adam and Eve in Qur'an 7:189, and how these defenses preserve prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
The verse about Adam and Eve 'associating partners' is interpreted by scholars as referring to later generations or minor disobedience.

This passage addresses a challenging verse in which Adam and Eve appear to commit shirk by naming their son 'Abd al-Harith. The text presents the story of Iblis's temptation and then provides multiple interpretive defenses that reconcile the verse with the doctrine of prophetic infallibility. As you read, note how the scholars use context, linguistic analysis, and the principle of choice to preserve the prophets' immunity from major sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Iblis tempting Hawwa
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the narrative of how Iblis deceived Eve and Adam's agreement to name the child 'Abd al-Harith.
The story sets up the apparent problem of shirk.
2
Understand the objection
Identify why the verse seems to accuse Adam and Eve of shirk. The pronoun 'they both associated' is taken to refer to them, and the story shows them complying with Iblis's request.
3
Examine the first answer
The first defense argues that the verse addresses Quraysh, not all humanity. 'One soul' is Qusayy, and 'her spouse' is a Qurayshi woman. Thus the shirk is naming children after idols, not Adam and Eve's action.
This interpretation avoids implicating Adam and Eve entirely.
4
Study the second answer
Even if the verse refers to Adam and Eve, the 'shirk' is an inclination to obey Iblis's whispering, not actual worship. This inclination is not under choice, so it is not a sin.
This preserves infallibility by distinguishing inner inclination from willful disobedience.
5
Consider other interpretations
The passage also suggests that the event occurred before prophethood, so it is a minor sin, not disbelief. Another view deletes the annexation, making 'they both associated' refer to their children.
These alternatives show the flexibility of scholarly interpretation to protect prophetic innocence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجعل منها زوجها [الأعراف: 189] يعني حواء ( ليسكن إليها فلما تغشاها حملت حملاً خفيفاً ﴾ [الأعراف: ۱۸۹] (الآية فإن الضمير في قوله : جعلا له شركاء راجع إليها إذ لم يتقدم ما يصلح لذلك سواهما والضمير في له لله سبحانه وتعالى فقد صدر عنه الإشراك وقصته أن حواء لما أثقلت أي حان وقت ثقل حملها جاءها إبليس في غير صورته وقال لها : لعل في بطنك بهيمة ؟ فقالت : ما أدري فلما ازداد ثقلها رجع إليها وقال : كيف تجدينك ؟ فقالت : أخاف مما خوفتني به فإني لا أستطيع القيام فقال : أرأيت لو دعوت الله أن يجعله إنساناً مثلي ومثل آدم تسميه باسمي ؟ فقالت : نعم ثم إنها حكت ما جرى بينهما لآدم فجعلا يدعوان الله لئن آتیتنا صالحاً أي ولداً سوياً لنكونن من الشاكرين فلما ولدت سوياً جاءها إبليس فقال : سميه باسمي قالت ما اسمك ؟ قال عبد الحارث وكان اسمه الحارث قسمته بعبد الحارث ورضي آدم بذلك . ) والجواب : إن أكثر المفسرين على أن الخطاب ) في خلقكم ( لقريش) وحدهم لا لبني آدم كلهم ) والنفس الواحدة قصي وجعل منها زوجها أي يجعلها عربية ) قرشية ( من جنسه ( لا أنه خلقها منه ) وإشراكهما ) بالله ( تسميتها أبناءهما بعبد مناف وعبد العزى وعبد الدار وعبد قصي ) والضمير في يشركون لهما ولا عقابهما وعلى هذا ليس الضمير في جعلا لآدم وزوجه وإن صح أنه لآدم وزوجه ( فأين الدليل على الشرك في الألوهية وفعله أي لعل الشرك المذكور في الميل إلى طاعة الشيطان وقبول وسوسته مع الرجوع عنه إلى الله تعالى ) بلا مطاوعة للشيطان في الفعل (وذلك) الميل المتفرع على الوسوسة (غير داخل تحت الاختيار) فلا يكون معصية وذنباً، أو لعله كان قبل النبوة) فإن قلت: قد مر امتناع الكفر على الأنبياء مطلقاً قلت : معنى إشراكهما بالله أنهما أطاعا إبليس في تسمية ولدهما بعبد الحارث كما مر في القصة، وليس ذلك كفراً بل ذنباً يجوز صدوره قبل النبوة وقد يقال : معنى جعلا أنه جعل أولادهما على حذف المضاف كما يدل عليه جمع الضمير في يشركون . قوله: ( وقد يقال: معنى جعلا إلخ) قيل: هذا قريب من الحق لأن أول الآية وهو قوله تعالى : هو الذي خلقكم من نفس واحدة ﴾ [الأنعام: ٢] ، يدل على ذلك التقدير كما يدل عليه ضمير الجمع في يشركون في آخر الآية .
And He made from her her spouse [al-A'raf 7:189] meaning Hawwa, that he might dwell with her. When he covered her, she conceived a light burden [al-A'raf 7:189] (the verse: the pronoun in His saying 'they associated partners with Him' refers to them since nothing else suitable precedes, and the pronoun in 'with Him' refers to Allah Almighty and Exalted, so shirk occurred from them. The story: when Hawwa became heavy, i.e., the time of her pregnancy's weight came, Iblis came to her in another form and said: 'Perhaps there is a beast in your womb?' She said: 'I do not know.' When her weight increased, he returned to her and said: 'How do you find yourself?' She said: 'I fear what you have frightened me with, for I cannot stand.' He said: 'What if you supplicated Allah to make it a human like me and like Adam, and you name it with my name?' She said: 'Yes.' Then she related what happened between them to Adam, and they both supplicated Allah: 'If You give us a righteous (child), i.e., a sound child, we will surely be among the grateful.' When she gave birth to a sound child, Iblis came to her and said: 'Name it with my name.' She said: 'What is your name?' He said: 'Abd al-Harith.' His name was al-Harith; she named it 'Abd al-Harith, and Adam agreed to that.) And the answer: Most of the interpreters agree that the address in 'He created you' is to Quraysh alone, not to all of Adam's children, and 'one soul' is Qusayy, and 'made from her her spouse' means He made her Arab, Qurayshi, from his kind, not that He created her from him. And 'their associating partners with Allah' means naming their sons 'Abd Manaf, 'Abd al-Uzza, 'Abd al-Dar, 'Abd Qusayy. And the pronoun in 'they associate partners' refers to them, not to their punishment. On this basis, the pronoun in 'they both associated' is not for Adam and his spouse, even if it is valid that it is for Adam and his spouse. So where is the proof of shirk in divinity? And its doing—perhaps the mentioned shirk is in inclination to obey shaytan and accepting his whispering while returning from it to Allah—without compliance with shaytan in the deed. And that inclination proceeding from whispering is not under choice, so it is not a sin and wrong, or perhaps it was before prophethood. If you say: It has already passed that disbelief is impossible for prophets absolutely, I say: The meaning of 'their associating partners with Allah' is that they obeyed Iblis in naming their son 'Abd al-Harith as passed in the story, and that is not disbelief but a sin that may occur before prophethood. And it may be said: The meaning of 'they both associated' is that He made their children, with the annexation deleted, as indicated by the plural pronoun in 'they associate partners'. (And it was said: the meaning of 'they both associated' etc.) It is said: This is close to the truth because the beginning of the verse—which is His saying: 'He is the one who created you from one soul' [al-An'am 6:2]—indicates that interpretation, as indicated by the plural pronoun in 'they associate partners' at the end of the verse.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding the apparent shirk of Adam and Eve based on verse 7:189. The story recounts how Iblis tempted Eve to name her child 'Abd al-Harith, and Adam agreed, leading to an interpretation that they associated partners with Allah. The answer provides multiple defenses: first, the verse refers to Quraysh, not to all humans; 'one soul' is Qusayy, and the 'spouse' is a Qurayshi woman, not Eve. Thus the shirk is naming children after idols, not associating worship partners with Allah. Second, even if it refers to Adam and Eve, the 'shirk' is obedience to shaytan in naming, which is not actual shirk in divinity but a sin, possibly before prophethood. Third, the inclination to obey shaytan's whispering is not under choice, so not a sin. The marginal note confirms that the verse's structure supports the interpretation that it refers to subsequent generations, not Adam and Eve themselves.

Defending Ibrahim's Statement 'This is My Lord'
Defends Ibrahim's apparent shirk by two defenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians defend Prophet Ibrahim's statement 'This is my Lord' against charges of shirk or lying.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's words were either a pre-prophethood mistake or a hypothetical argument, not actual shirk.

This passage addresses a potential objection to the prophetic status of Ibrahim: his statement 'This is my Lord' about a star. Critics argue it implies either shirk or lying. The author provides two defenses that protect Ibrahim's integrity. Read on to see how theologians reconcile this with his prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage that presents the objection: Ibrahim's statement could be shirk if believed, or a lie if not. Understand the dilemma.
Notice the two horns of the dilemma: belief leads to shirk, disbelief leads to lying.
2
Understand the first defense
The first defense argues that the statement was made before Ibrahim's prophethood, before his knowledge of God was complete. Thus, it was a lie before prophethood, which is not a sin for a prophet before prophethood.
This defense hinges on the timing of the statement relative to prophethood.
3
Understand the second defense
The second defense claims that Ibrahim spoke hypothetically, using a reductio ad absurdum argument to guide the Sabians. He did not actually believe the star was his Lord.
This is a common rhetorical device in Islamic theology called 'burhan al-khalf'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه أي ومن ذلك المجمل قصة إبراهيم عليه السلام وأظهر ما يوهم الذنب في قصته أمران: الأول: قوله في حق الكواكب: هذا ربي فإن كان ذلك عن اعتقاد كان شركاً وإلا كان كذباً. والجواب أن يقال: لا يخفى أنه أي هذا الكلام صدر عنه قبل تمام النظر في معرفة الله وكم بينه وبين النبوة إذ لا يتصور النبوة إلا بعد تمام ذلك النظر فلا إشكال إذ يختار أنه لم يعتقده فيكون كذباً صادراً قبل البعثة، ولك أن تقول: إنما قال ذلك على سبيل الفرض كما في برهان الخلف إرشاداً للصابئة إذ حاصل ما ذكره أن الكواكب لو كانت أرباباً كما تزعمون لزم أن يكون الرب متغيراً آفلاً وهو باطل.
And from that—i.e., from that summary—is the story of Ibrahim, peace be upon him. The most apparent things that suggest sin in his story are two: first: his statement regarding the stars: 'This is my Lord.' If that was from belief, it would be shirk; otherwise, it would be a lie. And the answer is to say: It is no secret that this speech issued from him before the completion of reflection in knowledge of Allah and the distance between him and prophethood, for prophethood cannot be conceived except after completion of that reflection, so there is no problem, since it is chosen that he did not believe it, so it would be a lie issued before his mission. And you may say: He only said it by way of hypothesis, as in the proof of the consequent, as guidance for the Sabians, since the gist of what he mentioned is that if the stars were lords as you claim, then the Lord would necessarily be changing and setting, which is false.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding Ibrahim's statement 'This is my Lord' about the stars. The objection is that if he believed it, it would be shirk; if not, it would be a lie. The answer provides two defenses: first, the statement occurred before his prophethood, before his knowledge of Allah was complete, so it was a lie before prophethood (which is permissible). Second, it was a hypothetical statement intended as a proof by way of reductio ad absurdum (برهان الخلف) to convince the Sabians that their claimed lords (stars) cannot be divine because they change and set.

Key Terms
هذا ربي
برهان الخلف
قبل النبوة
الكذب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibrahim genuinely believed the star was his Lord at that moment.
Correction: The passage explains it was either before his full knowledge of God (hence not shirk) or a hypothetical argument (a rhetorical proof).

Checkpoint
What is one of the defenses given for Ibrahim's statement about the stars?
A) It was a lie after prophethood
B) It was said by way of hypothesis as a proof
C) He actually believed it was his Lord
D) He was forced to say it

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's statement is defended as either a pre-prophethood utterance or a hypothetical argument for the Sabians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibrahim's statement was misunderstood by some. When have you judged someone's words harshly without knowing their full context or intention? Will you commit to seeking understanding before accusing?
?
Ibrahim used a hypothetical to teach, risking being misunderstood. When have you avoided a difficult conversation to protect your own reputation? Will you step out to guide others even if your words might be twisted?
?
The passage shows that before prophethood, Ibrahim's actions are not held to the same standard. Are you holding yourself to the standard of your current knowledge, or do you excuse your past ignorance while judging others for theirs? What will you change today?

Ibrahim's Request for Resurrection: Beyond Doubt to Eye-Certainty
Explains that Ibrahim's request was for eye-yakin, not due to doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ibrahim's request to see the dead resurrected was not due to doubt but a pursuit of a higher level of certainty.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's question sought eye-certainty, not because he doubted God's power.

One of the strongest objections against prophets is that Ibrahim's request to see the dead brought back to life seems to imply doubt in God's power. However, scholars explain that the request was actually a journey from knowledge-based certainty to direct experiential certainty, which grants greater tranquility and removes the heart's susceptibility to whisperings. Let's examine the answer provided in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage to understand the objection: Ibrahim's question implies doubt, which is kufr. Notice how the objection is framed.
2
Read the first answer
The answer offers two explanations. First, Ibrahim wanted 'ayn al-yaqin (eye-certainty) for greater tranquility. Understand the difference between 'ilm al-yaqin and 'ayn al-yaqin.
3
Read the second answer
The second explanation is that Ibrahim asked about the modality (kayfiyya), not the principle of resurrection. This clarifies he was not doubting.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
Ibn 'Abbas suggests Ibrahim wanted to confirm if he was the promised prophet who would revive the dead. This shifts the purpose from doubt to identity.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how the answers safeguard Ibrahim's faith while explaining his request. Complete the checkpoint to test your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: قوله رب: أرني كيف تحيي الموتى ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٦٠] والشك في قدرة الله على إحياء الموتى كفر. والجواب: إن ذلك السؤال لم يكن عن شك في الإحياء أو القدرة عليه بل في الآية تصريح بأنه طلبه لأن في عين اليقين من الطمأنينة ما ليس في علم اليقين فإن للوهم بإحداث الوساوس والدغادغ سلطانا على القلب عند علم اليقين دون عين اليقين، وقد يقال: إنما سأل عن كيفية الإحياء لا عنه لأن الإحاطة بالكيفية المفصلة أقوى وأرسخ من المعرفة الإجمالية المفضية إلى التردد بين الكيفيات المتعددة مع الطمأنينة في أصل الإحياء والقدرة عليه. هذا وقد قال ابن عباس: كان الله وعد أن يبعث نبياً يبعث نبياً يحيي بدعائه الموتى وذلك علامة أن الله تعالى قد اتخذه خليلاً فأراد إبراهيم أن يعلم أهو هو. ولا تحمل الآية على ما مر الشك في قدرة الله تعالى كفر وأنتم لا تقولون به فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا. قوله: ( وقد قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنه إلخ) المذكور في الأربعين أنه روي عن جعفر الصادق رضي الله عنه أن الله أوحى إليه أن اتخذ انسانا خليلا وعلامته أني أحيي وأميت بدعائه وهذه العبارة أظهر مما في الكتاب.
Second: his statement: 'My Lord, show me how You give life to the dead' [al-Baqarah 2:260]. And doubt in Allah's power to give life to the dead is disbelief. And the answer: That question was not from doubt about the reviving or power over it, but rather in the verse is an explicit statement that he requested it because in 'ayn al-yaqin (eye of certainty) there is tranquillity that is not in 'ilm al-yaqin (knowledge of certainty). For the imagination, by generating whisperings and disturbances, has authority over the heart at the level of 'ilm al-yaqin but not at 'ayn al-yaqin. And it may be said: He only asked about the modality of reviving, not about it [itself], because comprehension of the detailed modality is stronger and more firmly rooted than general knowledge leading to hesitation between multiple modalities, while having tranquillity about the principle of reviving and power over it. Moreover, Ibn 'Abbas said: Allah had promised that He would send a prophet by whose supplication the dead would be revived, and that was a sign that Allah had taken him as a friend (khalil). So Ibrahim wanted to know whether he was that one. And do not interpret the verse as indicating doubt in Allah's power, for that is disbelief, and you do not hold that, so whatever your answer is, it is our answer. His saying: (And Ibn 'Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, said, etc.) mentioned in al-Arba'in that it was narrated from Ja'far al-Sadiq, may Allah be pleased with him, that Allah revealed to him: 'Take a human as a friend, and the sign is that I give life and cause death by his supplication.' This wording is clearer than what is in the book.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding Ibrahim's request to see how Allah resurrects the dead. The objection is that asking 'how' implies doubt in God's power, which is kufr. The answer clarifies: the request was not due to doubt but to elevate from 'ilm al-yaqin (knowledge of certainty) to 'ayn al-yaqin (eye of certainty), which provides greater tranquillity and removes the imagination's influence. Alternatively, Ibrahim asked about the modality (kayfiyya), not the principle. The passage cites Ibn 'Abbas to show that Ibrahim wanted to confirm if he was the prophet promised to revive the dead, thus it was a request for identification, not a challenge. The marginal note adds that another narration states Allah revealed to Ibrahim that a sign of his khulla (friendship) would be the power to revive the dead.

عين اليقين
علم اليقين
كيفية الإحياء
الخلة

Misconception: Some claim Ibrahim doubted God's power to resurrect the dead, which would be disbelief.
Correction: The passage shows Ibrahim's request was for a higher level of certainty (eye-certainty) or to understand the modality, or to confirm his own prophetic identity, not due to doubt.

?

Defending Ibrahim's Statement: Attribution to Cause
Defends Ibrahim's statement as attribution to cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibrahim's statement 'Rather, the biggest of them did it' is defended as an attribution to the cause rather than a lie.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's words are not lies but figurative attributions to the real cause.

In this passage, the commentator addresses an objection to Prophet Ibrahim's statement that the biggest idol broke the smaller ones. The defense introduces the key kalam principle of 'attribution to cause' (الإسناد إلى السبب), explaining that the statement is not a literal lie but a rhetorical ascription of the action to its motivating factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the commentator presents the objection and then gives the main defense.
2
Identify the objection and answer
The objection is that Ibrahim said something false: 'Rather, the biggest of them did it.' The answer says it is an attribution to the cause. Understand what that means.
The cause is the people's excessive veneration of the big idol, which motivated Ibrahim to break them.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
The commentator also mentions that Ibrahim might have paused after 'their big one' and then said 'this, so ask them' while pointing to himself. This is considered a forced interpretation.
The word 'بل' (rather) supports the direct attribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتمسك به من قصة إبراهيم عليه الصلاة والسلام قوله بل فعله كبيرهم فإنه كذب، قلنا: هو من قبيل الإسناد إلى السبب فإن حامله على الكسر زيادة تعظيمهم لذلك الكبير، ومنه نظر نظرة في النجوم فقال: إني سقيم والنظر في علم النجوم حرام وحكمه بأنه سقيم كذب قلنا إن النظر في النجوم ليستدل بها على توحيد الله وكمال قدرته من أعظم الطاعات وأما ترتيب الحكم بالقسم على النظر فلعل الله تعالى أخبره بأنه إذا طلع النجم الفلاني فإنه يمرض. قوله: ( قلنا : هو إلخ) قيل: وأيضاً يجوز أن يكون قد وقف عند قوله: كبيرهم ثم ابتدأ بقوله: هذا فاسألوهم وعنى به نفسه لأن الإنسان أكبر من كل صنم ولا يخفى أنه تعسف سيما مع وجود بل. قوله: ( فلعل الله تعالى أخبره إلخ) وأيضاً يحتمل أن يكون سقيماً في ذلك الوقت فلم يكن قوله كذباً أو كان على تأويل الاستقبال كما في قوله تعالى: إنك ميت ﴾ [ الزمر: ٣٠] أو أراد سقم القلب من الحزن بسبب عناد القوم، وهاهنا بحث وهو أن إبراهيم عليه السلام صرح في حديث الشفاعة بصدور الكذب منه وأما أن المراد تصويره بصورة الكذب فذكره في مقام
And among what is clung to from the story of Ibrahim, peace and blessings be upon him, is his saying: 'Rather, the biggest of them did it.' This is a lie. We said: It is of the type of attribution to the cause, for the one who motivated him to break them was their excessive veneration of that big one. And from that is his looking a look at the stars and saying: 'Indeed, I am sick.' And looking into the science of the stars is forbidden, and his ruling that he is sick is a lie. We said: Looking into the stars to infer from them the oneness of Allah and the perfection of His power is among the greatest acts of obedience. As for ordering the ruling by oath to the looking, perhaps Allah informed him that when such-and-such star rises, he would become sick. His saying: (We said: it is he, etc.) It is said: It is also possible that he paused at his saying: 'their big one,' then began his saying: 'this, so ask them,' meaning himself, because a human is greater than any idol. And it is not hidden that this is forced, especially with the presence of 'rather.' His saying: (Perhaps Allah informed him, etc.) And also it is possible that he was sick at that time, so his statement was not a lie, or it was on the interpretation of the future, as in His statement: 'Indeed, you will die' [al-Zumar 39:30], or he meant sickness of the heart from grief due to the people's stubbornness. And here is a discussion: Ibrahim, peace be upon him, explicitly stated in the hadith of intercession that lying issued from him, and what is intended is its depiction in the form of a lie, so he mentioned it in the context.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage addresses two further objections regarding Ibrahim: (1) his statement 'Rather, the biggest of them did it' (which seems a lie). The answer says it is an attribution to the cause: his breaking of the idols was caused by their excessive veneration of the big idol, so the statement is a figurative ascription. (2) His claiming to be sick after looking at stars, which seems a lie and the act of looking at stars may be forbidden. The answer says looking at stars for guidance to Allah's oneness is permissible, and perhaps Allah informed him of a specific star's rising causing sickness. The marginal notes offer additional interpretations: for the first, that Ibrahim paused and then said 'this, so ask them' referring to himself, but this is forced. For the second, that Ibrahim may have actually been sick, or it was a future tense, or it means spiritual sickness. The passage ends with a note that Ibrahim himself admitted lying in the hadith of intercession, but that was a metaphorical depiction.

Key Terms
كذب
الإسناد إلى السبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibrahim told a lie by saying the biggest idol broke the others.
Correction: It is an attribution to the cause; the big idol's veneration caused the breaking, so the statement is figuratively true.

Checkpoint
How is Ibrahim's statement 'Rather, the biggest of them did it' defended?
A) It was a literal truth
B) It is an attribution to the cause of the breaking
C) He was forced to say it
D) He was joking

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's words are defended as an attribution to the cause, not a lie.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a small falsehood by calling it a 'white lie' or 'just a figure of speech'? After learning that Ibrahim's statement was not a lie but an attribution to cause, do you think you've been honest with yourself about your own excuses? Commit to one area where you will choose absolute truthfulness this week.
?
If you were in Ibrahim's position—alone against a whole community—would you have the courage to use wisdom and subtlety in your words rather than blunt confrontation? What does this teach you about the importance of intending the truth even when your words might be misunderstood? This week, pay attention to the intentions behind your own speech.
?
The real cause of Ibrahim's action was the people's veneration of the big idol. What are the 'big idols' in your life—things you venerate so much that they become the real cause of your actions? How can you follow Ibrahim's example of smashing those idols, even if you have to use indirect speech to do so?

Defending Ibrahim's Feigning Sickness
Defends Ibrahim's feigning sickness with multiple interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various defenses for Ibrahim's claim of sickness after looking at stars, including the permissibility of astrological inference for tawhid and the possibility of actual sickness or metaphorical interpretation.

Core Idea
Ibrahim's sickness claim is defended as either a permissible inference, a true statement, or a future tense figure.

This lesson examines a second apparent sin: Ibrahim looking at stars and saying 'I am sick' after. The commentary provides multiple defenses: looking at stars for guidance to Allah's oneness is commendable, perhaps Allah revealed he would become sick, or he was actually sick, or it was a future tense expression, or it referred to spiritual sickness. The lesson also touches on Ibrahim's own mention of lying in the hadith of intercession, which is interpreted metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the commentator piles up multiple defenses.
The passage is dense; take your time with each interpretation.
2
Identify the objection and first defense
The objection is that looking at stars is forbidden and claiming sickness is a lie. The first defense distinguishes forbidden astrology from permissible inference for tawhid.
3
Examine the additional possibilities
The commentator suggests: actual sickness, future tense, spiritual sickness, and the hadith of intercession is explained figuratively.
Each of these avoids attributing a real sin to Ibrahim.

Ibrahim's Request and the Certainty of Resurrection
Objection and answer regarding Ibrahim's request to see resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Prophet Ibrahim's request to see resurrection was not a sign of doubt but a pursuit of deeper certainty (ʿayn al-yaqīn).

Core Idea
Seeking to see does not mean doubting; it means seeking deeper certainty.

Prophet Ibrahim's question 'My Lord, show me how You give life to the dead' might seem to imply doubt in God's power. But Islamic theologians explain that he sought the eye of certainty beyond mere knowledge. This passage clarifies the distinction between types of certainty and defends prophetic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the flow: objection, then two answers, then a supporting report.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection
The objection is that Ibrahim's question might imply doubt in God's power to resurrect, which is unbelief. Locate the sentence that states this.
The objection is implicit but answered directly.
3
Understand the two answers
The first answer distinguishes between ʿilm al-yaqīn and ʿayn al-yaqīn. The second answer says Ibrahim asked about modality, not the resurrection itself. Read how each is argued.
The first answer uses the concept of wahm (imagination) causing misgivings.
4
Reflect on the Ibn Abbas report
Ibn Abbas said Ibrahim wanted to know if he was the prophet who would revive the dead by his supplication. This shows Ibrahim's motive was identification, not doubt.
The commentator adds a clearer version from Jaʿfar al-Ṣādiq.
5
Connect to prophetic infallibility
Prophets are infallible in conveying revelation and protected from major sins. This passage defends Ibrahim from the charge of doubting God's power.
This is a key lesson in ʿilm al-kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ) من الأمرين ( قوله رب : أرني كيف تحيي الموتى ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٦٠] والشك في قدرة الله ) على إحياء الموتى ) كفر و الجواب : إن ذلك السؤال لم يكن عن شك في الإحياء أو القدرة عليه بل ( في الآية تصريح بأنه طلبه لأن في عين اليقين من الطمأنينة ما ليس في علم اليقين فإن للوهم بإحداث الوساوس والدغادغ سلطانا على القلب عند علم اليقين دون عين اليقين)، وقد يقال : إنما سأل عن كيفية الإحياء لا عنه لأن الإحاطة بالكيفية المفصلة أقوى وأرسخ من المعرفة الإجمالية المفضية إلى التردد بين الكيفيات المتعددة مع الطمأنينة في أصل الإحياء والقدرة عليه، (هذا وقد قال ابن عباس : كان الله وعد أن يبعث نبياً يبعث نبياً يحيي بدعائه الموتى ) وذلك علامة أن الله تعالى قد اتخذه خليلاً ( فأراد ) إبراهيم ( أن يعلم أهو هو ) و ( كيف ) لا تحمل الآية على ما مر الشك في قدرة الله تعالى كفر وأنتم لا تقولون (به فما هو جوابكم فهو جوابنا، قوله : ( وقد قال ابن عباس رضي الله عنه إلخ) المذكور في الأربعين أنه روي عن جعفر الصادق رضي الله عنه أن الله أوحى إليه أن اتخذ انسانا خليلا وعلامته أني أحيي وأميت بدعائه وهذه العبارة أظهر مما في الكتاب.
The second of the two matters: His statement: 'My Lord, show me how You give life to the dead' [al-Baqarah 2:260]. Doubt about God's power to give life to the dead is unbelief. The answer: That question was not due to doubt about the reviving or about [God's] power over it, but rather the verse clearly states that he asked because in the eye of certainty (ʿayn al-yaqīn) there is a tranquility that is not present in the knowledge of certainty (ʿilm al-yaqīn); for the imagination (al-wahm) has authority over the heart during knowledge of certainty by producing whisperings and misgivings, which it does not have during the eye of certainty. And it is said: He only asked about the modality of reviving, not about the reviving itself, because detailed comprehensive knowledge of the modality is stronger and more firmly rooted than general knowledge that leads to hesitation among multiple modalities, while still having tranquility about the reviving itself and power over it. And indeed Ibn ʿAbbās said: God had promised that He would send a prophet who would revive the dead by his supplication, and that is a sign that God had taken him as a friend (khalīl). So Abraham wanted to know if he was that one. And [the verse] should not be interpreted as implying doubt about God’s power, which is unbelief – and you do not say that, so whatever your answer is, it is our answer. [Commentator: His statement: 'And indeed Ibn ʿAbbās said, etc.' mentioned in al-Arbaʿīn that it is narrated from Jaʿfar al-Ṣādiq, may God be pleased with him, that God revealed to him: 'Take a human being as a friend, and the sign of that is that I give life and death through his supplication.' And this wording is clearer than what is in the book.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the infallibility of prophets (ʿiṣmat al-anbiyāʾ). The objection is that Prophet Ibrahim's request 'Show me how You give life to the dead' implies doubt in God's power, which is unbelief (kufr). The answer explains that Ibrahim did not doubt but sought a higher level of certainty—the 'eye of certainty' (ʿayn al-yaqīn) as opposed to mere 'knowledge of certainty' (ʿilm al-yaqīn). The imagination can cause misgivings with knowledge of certainty, but direct vision provides greater tranquility. An alternative answer says he asked about the modality, not the fact. Then a report from Ibn ʿAbbās is cited, indicating that Ibrahim wanted to verify if he was the prophet who would revive the dead. The commentator adds a clarifying note about this report from Jaʿfar al-Ṣādiq.

Key Terms
عين اليقين
علم اليقين
عصمة الأنبياء
الوهم
الوساوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prophets such as Ibrahim can doubt God's power.
Correction: Prophets are infallible; Ibrahim's question was not out of doubt but to attain a higher degree of certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why did Ibrahim ask to see how God revives the dead?
A) He doubted God's power.
B) He wanted to attain eye of certainty (ʿayn al-yaqīn) for greater tranquility.
C) He was ignorant of God's power.
D) He was testing God.

Key Takeaway
Prophet Ibrahim's request to see resurrection was a pursuit of higher certainty, not a sign of doubt, upholding prophetic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it take for you to reach the tranquility of ʿayn al-yaqīn in a matter you are currently unsettled about?
?
Ibrahim (AS) did not settle for knowing that Allah can resurrect; he wanted to see how. What is one area of your faith where you have stopped at intellectual acceptance, avoiding the deeper, experiential certainty that would transform your life? What step will you take this week to move from ʿilm al-yaqīn to ʿayn al-yaqīn?
?
If you were offered a sign to remove all doubt from your heart, like Ibrahim, would you accept it? Or do you fear what that sign might demand of you? What is the whisper holding you back from asking Allah for deeper certainty about the truth of His promise?

Ibrahim's Statement and the Answer: Attribution to Cause
Objection and answer regarding Ibrahim's apparent lie about the idols.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent lie of Ibrahim is explained through the concept of attribution to cause (al-isnād ilā al-sabab).

Core Idea
Ibrahim's statement is not a lie but an attribution to the cause of the idol-breaking.

This lesson addresses an important objection regarding the sinlessness of prophets: Did Ibrahim lie when he said 'Rather, their chief did this'? The scholars provide a nuanced answer that protects the integrity of prophetic speech. We will explore the primary explanation of attribution to cause and a secondary alternative interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by an answer.
The passage includes a commentator's remark on an alternative view.
2
Understand the objection
Identify why critics consider Ibrahim's statement a lie. It appears to attribute the breaking of idols to the chief idol.
3
Learn the answer: attribution to cause
The main answer is that Ibrahim is not lying; he is attributing the act to the cause—the people's excessive veneration of the chief idol prompted the breaking.
This is a known rhetorical device in Islamic theology.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
The commentator mentions another view: Ibrahim may have paused after 'their chief' and then said 'this, so ask them' referring to himself. Note that this is considered far-fetched.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself why preserving prophetic sinlessness is important and how rhetorical devices help defend it.
This connects to the broader topic of 'isma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يتمسك به من قصة إبراهيم عليه الصلاة والسلام قوله بل فعله كبيرهم فإنه كذب، قلنا: هو من قبيل الإسناد إلى السبب فإن حامله على الكسر زيادة تعظيمهم لذلك الكبير، قوله : ( قلنا : هو إلخ) قيل: وأيضاً يجوز أن يكون قد وقف عند قوله: كبيرهم ثم ابتدأ بقوله : هذا فاسألوهم وعنى به نفسه لأن الإنسان أكبر من كل صنم ولا يخفى أنه تعسف سيما مع وجود بل .
And among what is used as proof from the story of Abraham, peace be upon him, is his statement: 'Rather, their chief did this' – for it is a lie. We say: It is of the type of attribution to the cause, for the one who prompted him to break [the idols] was their excessive veneration of that chief. [Commentator: His statement: 'We say: It is, etc.' It is said: Also, it is possible that he paused at his statement 'their chief' then began with his statement 'this, so ask them' and meant himself, because the human being is greater than any idol. And it is not hidden that this is far-fetched, especially with the presence of 'rather'.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that Ibrahim's statement 'Rather, their chief did this' is a lie. The answer is that this is a case of 'attribution to the cause' (al-isnād ilā al-sabab): the one who actually broke the idols was the people's excessive veneration of the chief idol, so Ibrahim attributed the act to that cause. The commentator adds another possible interpretation: Ibrahim may have paused after 'their chief' and then started a new sentence meaning 'this, so ask them' referring to himself, but this is considered far-fetched.

Key Terms
الإسناد إلى السبب
عصمة الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibrahim's statement is a literal lie that contradicts prophetic sinlessness.
Correction: It is a rhetorical device known as attribution to the cause, not a false statement.

Checkpoint
How do the commentators explain Ibrahim's statement 'Rather, their chief did this'?
A) He lied intentionally.
B) He attributed the act to the cause (the people's veneration of the chief idol).
C) He was confused about who broke the idols.
D) He meant that the chief idol did it literally.

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's statement is defended as an attribution to the cause, not a lie, thereby preserving prophetic sinlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ibrahim’s response reveals a trust in divine wisdom over human perception. What one accusation in your life might you need to reinterpret not as a lie but as a deeper truth? Will you ask Allah to show you that truth today?
?
The people’s excessive veneration of the idol led to its destruction. What ‘idol’—a person, habit, status, or comfort—do you venerate so much that it blinds you to Allah? Are you willing to allow that veneration to be shattered, even if it costs you? What step will you take this week to break that attachment?
?
The commentator dismissed a far-fetched interpretation even though it might have been easier to accept. Do you ever cling to interpretations of Quran or life that are convenient but weak? Will you submit to sound reasoning even when it humbles you? Pray for the courage to prefer truth over comfort.

Ibrahim's Claim of Sickness and Looking at Stars
Objection and answer regarding Ibrahim's claim to be sick.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defend the infallibility of prophets by interpreting the apparent lie of Prophet Ibrahim.

Core Idea
Prophetic infallibility demands creative interpretation of apparent sins.

One of the most challenging objections to prophetic infallibility is the story of Ibrahim claiming to be sick and looking at stars. Critics say he lied and practiced forbidden astrology. This lesson examines the classical defense: that Ibrahim's actions were either permissible or truthful when properly understood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening sentences of the passage which present the objection: Ibrahim looked at stars (forbidden) and claimed sickness (lie).
This sets up the challenge.
2
Examine the main answer
The response argues that looking at stars for guidance about God's power is worship, not forbidden. As for claiming sickness, perhaps God informed Ibrahim.
This is the primary defense.
3
Consider additional interpretations
The commentator adds three more possibilities: he was actually sick, it was a future tense, or it was metaphorical sickness of the heart.
These reinforce the point.
4
Understand the broader discussion
The passage ends with a reference to the hadith of intercession, hinting at deeper theological issues about prophetic statements.
This invites further study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه نظر نظرة في النجوم فقال : إني سقيم والنظر في علم النجوم حرام وحكمه بأنه سقيم كذب قلنا إن النظر في النجوم ليستدل بها على توحيد الله وكمال قدرته من أعظم الطاعات وأما ترتيب الحكم بالقسم على النظر فلعل الله تعالى أخبره بأنه إذا طلع النجم الفلاني فإنه يمرض، قوله : ( فلعل الله تعالى أخبره إلخ) وأيضاً يحتمل أن يكون سقيماً في ذلك الوقت فلم يكن قوله كذباً أو كان على تأويل الاستقبال كما في قوله تعالى : إنك ميت ﴾ [ الزمر : ٣٠] أو أراد سقم القلب من الحزن بسبب عناد القوم، وهاهنا بحث وهو أن إبراهيم عليه السلام صرح في حديث الشفاعة بصدور الكذب منه وأما أن المراد تصويره بصورة الكذب فذكره في مقام
And from it: he cast a glance at the stars and said, 'Indeed, I am sick.' Looking into the science of stars is forbidden, and his ruling that he was sick is a lie. We say: Looking at the stars to deduce the oneness of God and the perfection of His power is among the greatest acts of obedience. And here is a discussion: Abraham explicitly stated in the hadith of intercession that a lie issued from him, but the intended meaning is its portrayal in the form of a lie, and he mentioned it in the context...

This passage deals with the objection that Ibrahim's statement 'I am sick' is a lie, and his looking at the stars is forbidden. The answer explains that looking at stars to infer God's oneness is an act of worship, not forbidden. As for predicting sickness, perhaps God informed him of it. The commentator adds further possibilities: he was actually sick, or it was a future tense interpreted as present, or it was a sickness of the heart. The passage ends with a discussion about Ibrahim's confession of lying in the hadith of intercession, but the text is cut off.

B) He was actually sick or it was metaphorical.

?

Musa's Killing and Prophetic Infallibility
Objection and answer regarding Musa's killing and his statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the story of Musa's killing of the Coptic is reconciled with the doctrine of prophetic infallibility through the distinction of before and after prophethood.

Core Idea
Prophets are infallible after prophethood, not necessarily before.

The story of Prophet Musa striking and killing a Coptic man raises questions about prophetic sinlessness. Critics point to Musa's own words as admissions of wrongdoing. The traditional answer distinguishes between his state before and after prophethood, and interprets his statements as expressions of humility, not confessions of sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the three quotations from Musa that the objector uses: 'This is from the work of Satan,' 'I have wronged myself,' and 'I was among the astray.' Understand the claim that these indicate intentional murder.
The objector assumes that Musa's words are admissions of sin.
2
Analyze the answer
The answer gives two defenses: (1) The event happened before Musa's prophethood, so infallibility does not apply. (2) The killing could have been a mistake, and his words are signs of humility.
Note that the second defense is an alternative interpretation.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why timing matters for infallibility. Prophets are protected from sin only after receiving revelation.
This is a key principle in kalam.
4
Connect to broader doctrine
See how this objection and answer fit into the overall discussion of prophetic 'isma. Similar objections from other prophets are answered using similar reasoning.
The pattern is: if before prophethood, not an issue; if after, interpret as mistake or humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتمسك بها من وجوه الأول : قوله : فوكزه موسى فقضى عليه ولم يكن قتله ) لذلك القبطي ( بحق ) أي لم يكن مباحاً ولا على سبيل الخطأ بل كان قتل عمد عدوان ( لقوله : هذا من عمل الشيطان، وقوله : رب إني ظلمت نفسي، وقوله : فعلتها إذاً وأنا من الضالين الجواب أنه كان قبل النبوة ) وأيضا جاز أن يكون قتله خطأ وما صدر عنه من أقواله محمولا على التواضع وهضم النفس
And holding on to it from aspects: The first: His statement: 'So Moses struck him and finished him off,' and his killing of that Coptic was not by right – i.e., it was not permissible nor by mistake, but it was intentional transgressive murder – because of his statement: 'This is from the work of Satan,' and his statement: 'My Lord, indeed I have wronged myself,' and his statement: 'I did it then, and I was among the astray.' The answer: That was before prophethood. Also, it is possible that his killing was by mistake, and what issued from him of sayings is interpreted as humility and self-abasement.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection from the story of Moses regarding his killing of the Coptic. The objection claims it was intentional murder and that his subsequent statements ('this is from Satan', 'I have wronged myself', 'I was among the astray') indicate sin. The answer is twofold: (1) This occurred before prophethood, when he was not yet infallible. (2) It may have been a mistake, and his words are signs of humility and self-reproach, not admissions of sin.

?

Musa's Permission for Magic: Objections and Answers
Objection and answer regarding Musa's permission for magic display.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly responses to the objection that Prophet Musa's permission for magicians to display magic was forbidden.

Core Idea
Prophetic actions are always lawful and wise within their context.

This lesson tackles a critical objection to prophetic infallibility: how could Musa, a prophet of Allah, permit the forbidden act of magic? The text presents multiple answers, showing that his permission was either legal in his time, strategically necessary, or simply a challenge. Each response reinforces the principle that prophets act with perfect wisdom and in accordance with divine law.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection: Musa's permission for the magicians to throw their staffs (magic) is claimed to be forbidden because magic itself is forbidden. Identify the logical structure of the objection.
The objection is based on the assumption that magic is always forbidden and that permission implies approval.
2
Examine the first answer: abrogation
Study the first answer: magic was not forbidden in Musa's law because laws differ across times and prophets. Reflect on the concept of abrogation of divine laws.
This answer relies on the principle that Allah's commands may change over time.
3
Examine the second and third answers
The second answer: Musa knew they would throw anyway, so his permission showed indifference. The third answer: He needed their magic display to highlight his own miracle. Notice how these answers address the objection from different angles.
The third answer introduces the idea of a necessary prerequisite for an obligatory act.
4
Consider the fourth answer: conditional challenge
The fourth answer interprets Musa's words as a conditional challenge, similar to 'bring a surah like it if you are truthful.' This shifts the meaning from permission to a test.
This answer aligns with the Quranic style of challenging disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني أن أذن لهم في إظهار السحر لقوله : ألقوا ما أنتم ملقون ) وإظهاره حرام فيكون إذنه أيضاً حراماً . ( الجواب أنه ( أي إظهار السحر ( لم يكن حراماً حينئذ ( فإنه مما تختلف فيه الشرائع بحسب الأوقات ، ( أو علم ) موسى ( أنهم يلقون ( سواء ( أذن لهم أم لا بدليل ما أنتم ملقون ) فلا يكون ذلك الإذن حراما بل فيه قلة مبالاة بسحرهم ) أو أراد إظهار معجزته ) في عصاه وتلقفها لما أفكوه ( ولا يتم ذلك الإظهار في ذلك المقام ( إلا بذلك ) الإذن ( ) فكان واجباً (لكونه مقدمة للوجب ( أو أراد ألقوا ما أنتم ملقون (إن كنتم محقين نحو فأتوا بسورة من مثله إلى قوله : إن كنتم صادقين
The second: that he permitted them to display magic by his saying: 'Throw what you will throw' – and displaying magic is forbidden, so his permission would also be forbidden. The answer: That (i.e., displaying magic) was not forbidden at that time, for it is among things that differ across laws according to times. Or, Moses knew that they would throw whether he permitted them or not, as evidenced by 'what you will throw,' so that permission is not forbidden but rather contains indifference to their magic. Or, he intended to display his miracle in his staff swallowing what they fabricated, and that display in that situation would not be complete except by that permission, so it became obligatory as a prerequisite for the obligatory. Or, he intended 'Throw what you will throw' [meaning] 'if you are truthful,' like 'Then bring a surah like it' up to His saying 'if you are truthful.'
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that Musa's permission for the magicians to display magic was itself forbidden. The answer provides multiple justifications: (1) Magic was not forbidden in their law at that time, as laws differ. (2) Musa knew they would throw anyway, so his permission was merely indifferent. (3) He wanted to demonstrate his own miracle, so the permission was a necessary prerequisite. (4) His words could be interpreted as a conditional challenge, similar to 'bring a surah like it if you are truthful.'

Key Terms
السحر
اختلاف الشرائع
عصمة الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Musa's permission implies approval of magic in all contexts.
Correction: The permission was either lawful in his time, strategically indifferent, necessary for a greater miracle, or a conditional challenge. It does not imply that magic is permissible in general.

Checkpoint
What is NOT one of the answers regarding Musa's permission for magic?
A) Magic was not forbidden in their law.
B) Musa knew they would throw anyway.
C) Musa intended to show his miracle.
D) Musa was forced to permit it.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic actions are always lawful and wise, and apparent permissions that seem problematic are justified by context, necessity, or interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command or permission in Allah's revelation that seems difficult or contradictory to your moral instincts, do you pause and trust that there is a wisdom beyond your immediate understanding? How can Musa's permission—which on the surface appears to allow evil—train you to submit to Allah's complete knowledge even when you cannot see the full picture?
?
Have you ever judged someone's action as wrong without knowing the full context or the higher purpose behind it? This lesson shows that what appears to be a permission for magic was actually a necessary step for a greater miracle. Where in your life have you condemned a situation or person without understanding the divine wisdom at play, and how will you change that starting today?
?
The third answer says the permission became obligatory because it was a prerequisite for the obligatory. What 'permissions' in your own life—habits, relationships, or decisions—might God have allowed not because they are good in themselves, but because He is setting the stage for something greater? Are you willing to surrender your immediate judgments and trust His wise planning, even when it feels uncomfortable?

Defending Prophet Musa: The Grabbing of Harun
Objection and answer regarding Musa pulling Harun's head.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic scholars defended the prophet Musa's action of grabbing his brother Harun, and the tension between literal interpretation and the doctrine of prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Prophetic actions must be interpreted in light of their infallibility.

The story of Musa grabbing Harun by the head is a challenging passage for those who hold to the absolute infallibility of prophets. How could a prophet harm another prophet? This lesson presents the objection and the various answers given by theologians to reconcile the apparent action with the doctrine of 'isma.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection carefully. It sets up a dilemma: either Harun deserved it or Musa sinned. This forces a need for interpretation.
Notice the logical structure: if A then B, if not A then C.
2
Examine the first answer
The first answer reinterprets the action as drawing near to investigate, not to harm. This preserves both prophets' innocence.
This is a typical ta'wil (interpretive) approach.
3
Consider additional interpretations
Two more answers are given: calming Harun's distress, and Musa being overwhelmed so he treated Harun like himself. Each avoids harm.
These show the creativity of scholars in defending 'isma.
4
Reflect on al-Amidi's critique
Al-Amidi finds these interpretations far-fetched. This shows that even within the tradition, some found the defenses weak.
This humility is important for understanding kalam disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ( قوله ( وألقى الألواح وأخذ برأس أخيه يجره ،إليه هارون كان نبيا فإن كان له ذنب استحق به التأديب من موسى ( فذاك والمطلوب ولا فإيذاؤه بلا استحقاق ( ذنب) صدر عن موسى ) والجواب : أنه لم يكن ذلك ) الجر ( على سبيل الإيذاء بل كان يدنيه إلى نفسه ليتفحص منه حقيقة الحال في تلك الواقعة فخاف هارون أن يعتقد بنو إسرائيل خلافه أي يعتقدوا أنه يؤذيه وذلك لسوء ظنهم بموسى ( حتى أنه لما مات هارون في غيبتهم قالوا : إن موسى قتله، وقد أجيب أيضاً بأن موسی لما رأى جزع هارون واضطرابه لما جرى من قومه أخذه ليسكنه من قلقه كما يفعل الواحد منا إذا أراد إصلاح غضبان أو تسكين مصاب، وبأن موسى لما غالب عليه الهم واستيلاء الفكر أخذ برأس أخيه لا على طريقة الإيذاء بل كما يفعل الإنسان بنفسه عن عض يده وشفته وقبضه على لحيته إلا أنه نزل أخاه منزلة نفسه لأنه كان شريكه فيما يناله من خير أو شر، قال الآمدي : لا يخفى بعد هذه التأويلات وخروجها عن مذاق العقل
The third: His statement: 'And he threw the tablets and took his brother by the head, dragging him toward him.' Harun was a prophet. If he had a sin deserving discipline from Moses, then that is what is sought – otherwise, harming him without deserving is a sin from Moses. The answer: That dragging was not for harming, but he drew him near to examine from him the true state of affairs in that incident. Harun feared that the Children of Israel would think otherwise, i.e., that they would think he was harming him, due to their bad opinion of Moses, to the extent that when Harun died in their absence they said, 'Moses killed him.' And it was also answered that when Moses saw Harun's distress and agitation over what had happened from his people, he took him to calm his anxiety, just as one of us does when he wants to soothe an angry person or calm an afflicted one. And because Moses was overcome by worry and preoccupation, he took his brother's head not in a harmful way but as a person does to himself, biting his hand, lip, and grabbing his beard, except that he considered his brother like himself because he was his partner in whatever good or evil befell him. Al-Amidi said: The remoteness of these interpretations and their departure from the taste of reason are not hidden.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection regarding Moses taking his brother Harun by the head. The objection is that Harun was a prophet, so if he deserved punishment, that is one thing, but if not, then Moses harmed him unjustly. The answer gives multiple interpretations: (1) Moses drew him close to investigate the situation, not to harm. (2) Harun feared the people's bad opinion. (3) Moses intended to calm Harun's anxiety. (4) Moses was so overwhelmed that he acted like one biting his own hand, considering Harun as himself. Finally, al-Amidi criticizes these interpretations as far-fetched.

Key Terms
عصمة الأنبياء
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Musa intentionally hurt his brother Harun.
Correction: The interpretations show the action was not intended to harm but was for reassurance or due to being overwhelmed.

Checkpoint
Which scholar criticized the interpretations defending Musa's action?
A) Ibn Abbas
B) Al-Amidi
C) Al-Ghazali
D) Al-Jurjani

Key Takeaway
The story of Musa grabbing Harun is reconciled with prophetic infallibility through multiple interpretations, though some scholars find these interpretations unconvincing.

Musa's Statement to al-Khadir: Objection and Answer
Objection and answer regarding Musa's comment 'You have done a strange thing.'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological responses to the objection that Prophet Musa's statement to al-Khadir could be a lie, and how these responses defend prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Prophetic statements are protected; apparent missteps are explained contextually.

This lesson tackles a specific objection raised against prophetic infallibility: the statement of Prophet Musa to al-Khadir, 'You have done a strange thing.' Critics argue this shows Musa made an inappropriate or false statement. Theologians provide several interpretations to show that this does not violate infallibility, including the possibility of a permissible lie in dire circumstances or that the event occurred before his prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the objection and the responses that follow.
2
Identify the objection
The passage starts with 'The fourth: His statement...' which is the objection. What exactly is being objected to?
The objection is that Musa's comment could be a lie.
3
Examine the first answer
The first response suggests it is a 'permissible lie' (الكذبات المباحة) to escape a tyrant. Understand the concept of permissible deception in Islam.
This is a limited exception for dire situations.
4
Examine the commentator's answers
The commentator adds two more possibilities: the event occurred before Musa's prophethood, or that this Musa is a different person. See how these preserve infallibility.
5
Reflect on infallibility
Consider how each interpretation ensures that no sin or falsehood is attributed to a prophet, thus maintaining their 'isma (infallibility).
These explanations show the care theologians take to defend prophetic integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : قوله ( أي قول موسى للخضر ولقد جئت شيئاً إمراً ﴾ [الكهف: ٧١] أنه ليس كذباً منه لا يصلح منه عليه السلام قيل ويمكن حمله على أنه من : الكذبات المباحة للتخلص عن يد الظالم والتولي عن مجتمعهم، قوله : أي قول موسى عليه السلام للخضر) قيل : يحتمل أن يكون هذا من موسى قبل النبوة لأنه بعدما أرسل لم يكن له وقت يصرفه في تعلم العلم، بل هو المتكلم من العليم الحكيم وقيل : إن موسى عليه السلام هاهنا غير موسى النبي عليه السلام إلا أن يكون صاحبه غير الخضر،
The fourth: His statement – i.e., the statement of Moses to al-Khadir: 'You have certainly done a strange thing' [al-Kahf 18:71]. That it is not a lie from him, [but] such is not proper from him, peace be upon him. It is said: It can be interpreted as being from the permissible lies to escape from the hand of a tyrant and to turn away from their society. [Commentator: His statement: i.e., the statement of Moses, peace be upon him, to al-Khadir. It is said: It is possible that this was from Moses before prophethood, because after he was sent he had no time to spend on learning knowledge; rather, he is the speaker from the All-Knowing, the Wise. And it is said: That Moses here is other than the prophet Moses, peace be upon him, unless his companion was other than al-Khadir.]
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth objection regarding Moses' comment to al-Khadir: 'You have certainly done a strange thing.' The objection is that this statement could be considered a lie or inappropriate. The answer suggests it might be a permissible lie to escape a tyrant. The commentator adds that this incident may have occurred before Moses' prophethood, or that the Moses in this story is a different Moses, not the prophet. These interpretations aim to preserve prophethood's infallibility.

Key Terms
الكذبات المباحة
عصمة الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That every statement by a prophet must be literally true; permissible deception is not allowed.
Correction: Islamic theology acknowledges limited exceptions for permissible deception in dire situations, such as saving life or escaping tyranny.

Checkpoint

B) It was before prophethood.
C) Musa was confused.

Key Takeaway
The infallibility of prophets is defended by interpreting potentially problematic statements within permissible contexts such as necessity, pre-prophethood, or mistaken identity.

First Objection to Moses' Infallibility and Its Answer
First objection to Moses' infallibility and its answer on temporal grounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against Moses' infallibility regarding the killing of the Copt and how theologians answer it by distinguishing between pre-prophethood and post-prophethood.

Core Idea
Moses' statements before prophethood do not affect his prophetic infallibility.

This passage presents a common objection to the doctrine of prophetic infallibility ('isma). Critics point to Moses' actions and words in the story of the Copt as evidence of sin. However, the response clarifies the context: these events occurred before Moses was granted prophethood, and his expressions are understood as humility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector uses three quotes from Moses to argue that he committed a wrongful murder and admitted sin. Note the phrases: 'from the work of Satan', 'I have wronged myself', 'I was of the astray ones'.
2
Notice the structure of the answer
The answer gives two distinct arguments: first, the event was before prophethood; second, the killing could have been a mistake and the words are expressions of humility.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
This illustrates the important distinction between the state before and after prophethood. Actions and words before prophethood do not impact the infallibility of the prophet after revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : قوله : فوكزه موسى فقضى عليه ولم يكن قتله لذلك القبطي بحق أي لم يكن مباحاً ولا على سبيل الخطأ بل كان قتل عمد عدوان لقوله : هذا من عمل الشيطان، وقوله : رب إني ظلمت نفسي، وقوله : فعلتها إذاً وأنا من الضالين الجواب أنه كان قبل النبوة وأيضا جاز أن يكون قتله خطأ وما صدر عنه من أقواله محمولا على التواضع وهضم النفس
The first: His statement: 'So Moses struck him and finished him off, and his killing of that Copt was not by right' meaning it was not permissible nor by mistake, rather it was intentional wrongful murder, because of his statement: 'This is from the work of Satan,' and his statement: 'My Lord, I have wronged myself,' and his statement: 'I did it then, and I was of the astray ones.' The answer: That was before prophethood. Also it is possible that his killing was a mistake, and what issued from him of statements are interpreted as humility and self-deprecation.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the infallibility of Moses (peace be upon him). The objector cites three things: that Moses killed the Copt unjustly (as a deliberate act), that he called it a work of Satan, that he said he wronged himself, and that he said he was of the astray ones. All these imply sin. The answer first argues that these acts occurred before Moses received prophethood, so they are not relevant to his prophetic infallibility. Additionally, the killing could have been a mistake, and his words are taken as expressions of humility, not as admissions of sin.

Key Terms
عصمة
الأنبياء
قتل العمد
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses' statement 'I was of the astray ones' implies he was sinful as a prophet.
Correction: The answer shows this refers to a time before prophethood and is interpreted as humility.

Checkpoint

A) The killing was a mistake
B) It happened before Moses became a prophet
C) Moses was commanded to kill
D) The Copt was an enemy

Key Takeaway
Events before prophethood do not undermine the infallibility of prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage shows that even a prophet's pre-prophethood mistakes are not held against him. How does this understanding of divine mercy affect your relationship with Allah when you stumble after having come to faith? Are you still making excuses for your sins, or are you ready to truly repent and move forward without looking back?

Moses' Permission for Magic: Objection and Answers
Objection and answers on Moses allowing magic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that Moses' permission to show magic contradicts prophethood, and the multiple theological answers that resolve it.

Core Idea
Moses' command to cast magic was not sin; it was contextual, necessary, or a challenge.

The second objection concerns Moses telling the magicians to cast their staffs, which appears to be permitting magic. Since magic is forbidden, how could a prophet allow it? The scholars offer several sophisticated answers that consider abrogation, divine knowledge, and the purpose of miracles. This passage walks through them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premise
The objection states: Moses said 'Cast what you are going to cast,' and since showing magic is forbidden, his permission is also forbidden. Understand the logical structure.
2
Identify the four answers
The answers are introduced by 'أو' (or). List each one: (1) magic was not forbidden then, (2) Moses knew they would cast anyway, (3) permission was necessary for the miracle, (4) the command was actually a challenge.
Notice the range: from abrogation to rational necessity.
3
Examine the theological reasoning
Each answer relies on a different principle: abrogation of laws, divine foreknowledge, means to an obligatory end, and implicit condition in commands.
These show how kalam defends prophethood.
4
Reflect on the defense of prophethood
Consider how these answers collectively refute the accusation without compromising Moses' prophethood. The objection fails because it ignores context and divine wisdom.
This connects to the broader topic of miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني أن أذن لهم في إظهار السحر لقوله : ألقوا ما أنتم ملقون وإظهاره حرام فيكون إذنه أيضاً حراماً . الجواب أنه أي إظهار السحر لم يكن حراماً حينئذ فإنه مما تختلف فيه الشرائع بحسب الأوقات، أو علم موسى أنهم يلقون سواء أذن لهم أم لا بدليل ما أنتم ملقون فلا يكون ذلك الإذن حراما بل فيه قلة مبالاة بسحرهم أو أراد إظهار معجزته في عصاه وتلقفها لما أفكوه ولا يتم ذلك الإظهار في ذلك المقام إلا بذلك الإذن فكان واجباً لكونه مقدمة للوجب أو أراد ألقوا ما أنتم ملقون إن كنتم محقين نحو فأتوا بسورة من مثله إلى قوله : إن كنتم صادقين
The second: That he gave them permission to show magic by his saying: 'Cast what you are going to cast.' And showing it is forbidden, so his permission is also forbidden. The answer: That showing magic was not forbidden at that time, for it is among things that differ in laws according to times. Or Moses knew that they would cast whether he gave permission or not, evidence being 'what you are going to cast', so that permission is not forbidden, rather it shows lack of concern for their magic. Or he intended to show his miracle in his staff swallowing what they faked, and that showing is not accomplished in that situation except by that permission, so it became obligatory because it is a prerequisite for what is obligatory. Or he intended 'Cast what you are going to cast, if you are truthful' as in 'bring a surah like it' up to his saying 'if you are truthful'.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection: Moses ordered the magicians to cast their staffs (showing magic), and since magic is forbidden, ordering it should also be forbidden. The answer offers multiple possibilities: (1) The prohibition of magic may have been abrogated or differed in previous laws—it might have been permissible at that time. (2) Moses knew the magicians would cast regardless, so his command was not an endorsement but a lack of concern. (3) His intention was to display the miracle of his staff, which required the magicians to cast first, so the permission was necessary and thus obligatory as a means to a duty. (4) Alternatively, his words 'cast what you are going to cast' might be a challenge like 'bring a surah' to prove falsehood, not a genuine permission.

Key Terms
سحر
إذن
شرائع
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A prophet cannot allow something forbidden under any circumstance.
Correction: The answers show that rulings vary across laws, and actions can be permissible due to context (e.g., necessity, foreknowledge, or conditionality).

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the answers given for why Moses' permission to show magic was not sinful?
A) Magic was not forbidden in their law
B) Moses knew they would cast anyway
C) Moses intended to teach them magic
D) The permission was necessary for the miracle

Key Takeaway
Moses' permission for magic was not a sin; it was either permissible in that law, inconsequential due to foreknowledge, necessary for the miracle, or a challenge to the magicians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the objection against Moses, did you feel any doubt about his prophethood? How did the answers strengthen your trust in the perfection of Allah's messengers? Be honest: did you previously assume that a prophet could never permit something that seems wrong? Will you now pause before judging the actions of the prophets or even your own scholars?
?
The fourth answer interprets Moses' command as a challenge: 'Cast if you are truthful.' Think of a time when you made a statement that could be misunderstood. Were you willing to explain or did you allow the misunderstanding to persist? In light of this lesson, how can you be more careful about the intentions behind your words?
?
Consider the principle of abrogation: laws change by time. Is there an area of your life where you have been clinging to a ruling that might not apply to your situation, or vice versa, ignoring a clear command because it doesn't suit you? How can this lesson help you balance between timeless truths and temporal applications?

Defending Prophet Moses: The Third Objection About Grabbing Aaron's Head
Objection to Moses grabbing Aaron's head with multiple defenses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defended Prophet Moses's action of grabbing Aaron's head, showing that prophetic acts can be interpreted within a context of care rather than harm.

Core Idea
Prophetic actions are judged by intent, not appearance.

One of the objections raised against the sinlessness of prophets is the incident where Moses grabbed Aaron by the head. Critics argue that if Aaron was innocent, this act was hurtful and thus sinful. Scholars provide several interpretations to show that Moses's action was not hurtful but rather a gesture of concern. This lesson examines the objection and the nuanced responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the critic's argument that Moses's action implies sin if Aaron was innocent.
The objector uses the Quranic verse as evidence.
2
Examine the first answer
Notice the interpretation that Moses drew Aaron close to investigate, not to hurt, and Aaron feared misunderstanding of the people.
This answer focuses on Moses's intention and Aaron's perception.
3
Consider the further answers
See how Moses's act is likened to calming distress or treating oneself, indicating it was a natural reaction.
These answers compare Moses's action to common human behavior.
4
Reflect on al-Amidi's remark
Understand that even if interpretations seem far-fetched, the objection is considered weak, hence the need for such interpretations.
Al-Amidi's comment is itself part of the defense.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Grasp the principle that prophetic actions must be evaluated based on intention and context, not solely on outward appearance.
This reinforces the doctrine of prophetic sinlessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قوله وألقى الألواح وأخذ برأس أخيه يجره إليه هارون كان نبيا فإن كان له ذنب استحق به التأديب من موسى فذاك والمطلوب وإلا فإيذاؤه بلا استحقاق ذنب صدر عن موسى والجواب : أنه لم يكن ذلك الجر على سبيل الإيذاء بل كان يدنيه إلى نفسه ليتفحص منه حقيقة الحال في تلك الواقعة فخاف هارون أن يعتقد بنو إسرائيل خلافه أي يعتقدوا أنه يؤذيه وذلك لسوء ظنهم بموسى حتى أنه لما مات هارون في غيبتهم قالوا : إن موسى قتله، وقد أجيب أيضاً بأن موسى لما رأى جزع هارون واضطرابه لما جرى من قومه أخذه ليسكنه من قلقه كما يفعل الواحد منا إذا أراد إصلاح غضبان أو تسكين مصاب، وبأن موسى لما غالب عليه الهم واستيلاء الفكر أخذ برأس أخيه لا على طريقة الإيذاء بل كما يفعل الإنسان بنفسه عن عض يده وشفته وقبضه على لحيته إلا أنه نزل أخاه منزلة نفسه لأنه كان شريكه فيما يناله من خير أو شر، قال الآمدي : لا يخفى بعد هذه التأويلات وخروجها عن مذاق العقل
The third: His statement: 'And he threw down the tablets and took hold of his brother's head, dragging him toward him.' Aaron was a prophet. If he had a sin deserving discipline from Moses, then that is what is sought; but if not, then hurting him without deserving is a sin from Moses. The answer: That dragging was not for the purpose of hurting; rather he was drawing him close to himself to investigate from him the reality of the situation in that incident. Aaron feared that the Children of Israel would think otherwise, i.e., that they would think he was hurting him, due to their bad opinion of Moses, so much so that when Aaron died in their absence they said, 'Moses killed him.' It was also answered that when Moses saw Aaron's distress and anxiety over what his people had done, he took him to calm his agitation, just as one of us does when wanting to calm an angry person or soothe an afflicted one. And that Moses, when overcome by worry and preoccupation of thought, took hold of his brother's head not in a hurtful way, but as a person does to himself by biting his hand, biting his lip, or grasping his beard—except that he placed his brother in the position of himself because he was his partner in whatever good or evil befell him. Al-Amidi said: The distance of these interpretations and their departure from the taste of reason is not hidden.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection: Moses grabbed Aaron's head and dragged him, which seems hurtful toward a prophet. The objector argues that if Aaron deserved punishment, then fine, but if not, Moses committed a sin. The answer provides several interpretations: (1) Moses was not hurting Aaron but pulling him close to inquire about the situation. Aaron feared people's misunderstanding, so he recoiled. (2) Moses saw Aaron's distress and took him to calm him, like one calms an angry person. (3) Moses was himself overwhelmed and acted as one does to oneself in distress—he treated Aaron as part of himself. The commentator Al-Amidi notes that these interpretations are far-fetched and not rationally palatable, which itself becomes part of the defense (possibly showing that the objection is weak and must be answered with such interpretations).

Key Terms
أذى
تأويل
هارون
الآمدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any apparent harsh treatment between prophets is automatically sinful.
Correction: The passage shows that such actions can be justified by context and intent, and are not necessarily signs of sin.

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason given for Moses grabbing Aaron's head?
A) To punish Aaron for allowing idolatry
B) To draw him close and investigate the situation
C) To express anger at Aaron's failure
D) To physically assault him

Key Takeaway
Prophetic actions must be interpreted with consideration of intent and context, not solely on outward appearance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
