The Fourth Objection: Moses' Words to al-Khidr Explained
Objection and multiple answers, with emphasis on outward interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defend the prophet Moses from the accusation of lying by interpreting his words as reflecting outward appearance rather than inner reality, and by considering other contextual explanations.

Core Idea
Moses spoke from outward perception; the act was not wrong in reality.

This passage presents a fourth objection: Moses called al-Khidr's actions 'awful' and 'wrong', but since those actions were commanded by God, they were not actually wrong. Does this mean Moses lied or erred? The text offers several responses, primarily that Moses meant 'wrong' from the apparent perspective or as a synonym for 'strange', and that the act was not wrong in truth. Additional interpretations include permissible lies, pre-prophethood timing, and identification of a different Moses or companion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its phrasing
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection uses Quranic verses (18:71, 18:74) and states that Moses' words appear to accuse al-Khidr of wrongdoing.
The key terms are 'إمراً' (awful) and 'نكراً' (wrong).
2
Identify the initial responses
The text lists several quick answers: permissible lies to escape oppressors, that it was before Moses' prophethood, or that this Moses is a different person. Notice how each attempts to avoid attributing error to a prophet.
These are weaker answers; the main answer follows.
3
Focus on the main answer: outward vs. reality
The core response is that Moses spoke about the outward appearance of the act, not its true reality. The word 'wrong' can also mean 'strange' in Arabic. Since al-Khidr acted by God's command, the act was not wrong in reality.
This answer uses the concepts of 'ظاهر' (outward) and 'تأويل' (interpretation).
4
Consider the historical note
The passage ends with a historical discussion about al-Khidr's timeline. Some say he was not contemporary with Moses; others provide a different narrative. Reflect on how this affects the objection.
This note shows that scholarly debate includes historical verification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : قوله : أي قول موسى للخضر ولقد جئت شيئاً إمراً ﴾ [الكهف: ٧١] أنه ليس كذباً منه لا يصلح منه عليه السلام قيل ويمكن حمله على أنه من : الكذبات المباحة للتخلص عن يد الظالم والتولي عن مجتمعهم قوله : أي قول موسى عليه السلام للخضر) قيل : يحتمل أن يكون هذا من موسى قبل النبوة لأنه بعدما أرسل لم يكن له وقت يصرفه في تعلم العلم، بل هو المتكلم من العليم الحكيم وقيل : إن موسى عليه السلام هاهنا غير موسى النبي عليه السلام إلا أن يكون صاحبه غير الخضر، و شيئاً نكراً ﴾ [الكهف : ٧٤] وذلك الفعل لم يكن منكراً فكل كلام موسى خطأ وقد يقال : إن كان فعل الخضر منكراً فذاك وإلا كان كاذباً قلنا : أراد منكراً موسی من حيث الظاهر على معنى أن من نظر إلى ظاهر هذه الواقعة ولم يعرف حقيقتها حكم عليها بأنها شيء منكر أو أراد عجباً، فإن من رأى شيئاً عجيباً جداً فإنه قد يقول : هذا شيء منكر، وفعل الخضر لما كان بأمر الله لم يكن منكراً في الحقيقة، والتواريخ نطقت بأن الخضر المعهود لم يكن في زمن موسى بل ولد بعد وفاته بسنتين والمذكور في تفسير القاضي وغيره أن موسى عليه السلام خطب بعد هلاك القبط ودخوله مصر خطبة بليغة فأعجب بها فقيل له : هل تعلم أحداً أعلم منك فقال : لا فأوحى الله تعالى إليه بل عبدنا خضر وهو بمجمع البحرين وكان الخضر في أيام أفريدون الملك وكان على مقدم ذي القرنين الأكبر وبقي إلى يوم موسى عليه السلام والله أعلم
The fourth: His statement, i.e., the statement of Moses to al-Khidr: 'Indeed you have brought something awful' (18:71) — that it is not a lie from him, but it is not proper from him, peace be upon him. It was said: It can be interpreted as one of the permissible lies to escape from the hand of an oppressor and to leave their company. His statement, i.e., the statement of Moses to al-Khidr: It was said: It is possible that this was from Moses before prophethood, because after he was sent, he did not have time to spend on learning knowledge; rather, he is the speaker from the All-Knowing, the Wise. And it was said: The Moses here is other than the prophet Moses, unless his companion is other than al-Khidr. And 'something wrong' (18:74) — and that act was not wrong, so all of Moses' speech is false. And it may be said: If the act of al-Khidr was wrong, then that is it, otherwise he was lying. We said: Moses meant 'wrong' from the outward perspective, meaning that whoever looks at the outward of this incident and does not know its reality would judge it as a wrong thing. Or he meant 'strange', for whoever sees something very strange may say: This is a wrong thing. And the act of al-Khidr, since it was by the command of God, was not wrong in reality. And the histories have stated that the well-known al-Khidr was not in the time of Moses; rather, he was born two years after his death. And it is mentioned in the exegesis of al-Qadi and others that Moses, after the destruction of the Copts and his entry into Egypt, delivered an eloquent sermon and was admired for it, so it was said to him: Do you know anyone more knowledgeable than you? He said: No. So God revealed to him: Rather, Our servant al-Khidr, and he is at the junction of the two seas. And al-Khidr was in the days of Afridun the king, and he was at the forefront of Dhu al-Qarnayn the Greater, and he remained until the time of Moses, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection: Moses called al-Khidr's actions 'awful' and 'wrong', but since those actions were by God's command, they were not actually wrong, so Moses either lied or erred. The answer offers several interpretations: (1) Moses meant 'awful' as a permissible lie to save himself from the oppressor? Actually the text says it is not a lie but not proper, then mentions the interpretation of a permissible lie to escape oppressors. (2) It might have been before prophethood. (3) The Moses here might be a different person. (4) Regarding 'wrong', Moses meant it from the outward appearance, not the true reality—anyone seeing it would think it wrong, but in truth it was good. Alternatively, 'wrong' can mean 'strange' in Arabic. The passage also includes a historical note: the well-known al-Khidr was not contemporary with Moses, but some say he was, giving a different narrative. The commentator reconciles by citing exegesis that al-Khidr lived from the time of Afridun to Moses.

الخضر
كذب
ظاهر
تأويل
إمر
نكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Moses told a lie or made a mistake by calling al-Khidr's action 'wrong'.
Correction: The passage explains that Moses spoke from the outward perspective; his words were not false because the action appeared wrong to any observer. Alternatively, 'wrong' can mean 'strange'.

Checkpoint
According to the main answer in the passage, what did Moses mean when he called al-Khidr's act 'something wrong'?
A) It was literally sinful
B) It appeared wrong from the outward perspective
C) He was lying
D) He did not know it was from God

Key Takeaway
Moses' words do not imply he lied; they reflect a perspective-based judgment that is compatible with prophetic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you confidently judged someone's action as 'wrong' without knowing the full reality behind it? How does Moses' example push you to pause and consider that God's wisdom might be hidden? Will you commit to asking for the unseen context before condemning another believer's deed this week?
?
The author says al-Khidr's act was by God's command and therefore not wrong. Is there an event in your own life that seemed 'awful' but you later realized was for your good? How can you train your heart to trust God's plan even when your eyes see only the outward 'awfulness'? What specific situation will you surrender to God's wisdom right now?
?
Moses spoke from his limited perspective. Are there areas where you treat your limited understanding as absolute truth—especially regarding matters of religion or others' intentions? What would it mean for you today to humbly say, 'This seems wrong to me, but I acknowledge that I may not see the whole picture'? Write one practical step you will take to embrace that humility.

Defense of David's Infallibility Against the Fabricated Story
Refutation of the fabricated story of David and Uriah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the story of David and Uriah's wife is rejected as incompatible with the Quranic praises and prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
David's Quranic praises disprove the fabricated story of Uriah's wife.

The story of David coveting Uriah's wife has been circulated by some narrators. However, the great praises God gave David in the Quran—such as his strength in religion, his constant return to God, and his wisdom—stand in stark opposition to such vile acts. This passage systematically demonstrates the incompatibility, proving the story is a fabrication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation slowly. Notice how the author lists specific Quranic praises and then argues against the story.
Pay attention to the logical flow: claim, evidence, conclusion.
2
Identify the four praises
The passage lists four attributes of David: power in religion, awwab (returning to God), subjugation of mountains and birds, and wisdom with decisive speech. Find each one and see why each is incompatible with the alleged sins.
These praises are taken from the Quran before the 'story of the ewe'.
3
Understand the alternative explanation
The author concludes that the Quranic story (the ewe) does not refer to David's sin but to a plot against him. Consider how this resolves the apparent conflict between the praises and the story.
This shows that the story of Uriah's wife is a later fabrication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه قصة داود عليه السلام وهي أنه طمع في امرأة أوريا فقصد قتله بإرساله إلى الحرب مرة بعد أخرى وهذه القصة على الوجه الذي اشتهرت به مختلقة أي مفتراة للحشوية إذ لا يليق إدخال الذم الشنيع في أثناء المدائح العظام يعني أن الله تعالى مدح داود قبل قصة النعجة بأوصاف كمالية منها أنه ذو الأيدي أي القوة وأراد القوة في الدين لأن القوة في الدنيا كانت حاصلة لملوك الكفار ولم يستحقوا بها مدحاً، والقوة في الدين هي العزم الشديد على أداء الواجبات وترك المنكرات فكيف يوصف بها من لم يملك منع نفسه عن الميل إلى الفجور والقتل، ومنها أنه أواب أي رجاع إلى ذكر الله فكيف يتصور منه أن يكون مواظباً على القصد إلى أعظم الكبائر، ومنها أنه سخر له الجبال يسبحن معه بالعشي والإشراق، وسخر له الطير محشورة كل له أواب أفترى أنه سخر له هذه الأشياء ليتخذها وسائل إلى القتل والزنا؟ ومنها أنه أوتي الحكمة وفصل الخطاب والحكمة اسم جامع لكل ما يبنغي علماً وعملاً فكيف يعقل أنه اتصف بالحكمة مع إصراره على ما يستنكف عنه أخبث الشياطين من مزاحمة أتباعه في الزوج والمنكوحة، ومدحه أيضاً بعد قصة النعجة بأنه جعله خليفة في الأرض وهذا من أجل المدائح وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم أن تحمل القصة على أنها إشارة إلى القصة المشهورة في حق داود عليه السلام، بل تصور قوم قصره للإيقاع به فلما رأوه مستيقظا اخترع أحدهم الخصومة المذكورة في القرآن وزعموا أنهم إنما قصدوه لأجلها لا لسوء به من قتل
And from it is the story of David, peace be upon him: that he coveted the wife of Uriah and intended to kill him by sending him to war time after time. This story, as it is famous, is fabricated, i.e., invented by the Hashwiyya, for it is not fitting to insert vile blame in the midst of great praises. That is, God Almighty praised David before the story of the ewe with perfect attributes, among them that he was 'possessor of hands' i.e., power, and He meant power in religion, because power in the world was possessed by the kings of the disbelievers and they did not deserve praise for it. Power in religion is strong resolve to perform obligations and abandon wrongdoings; so how can one be described with it who could not prevent himself from inclining to immorality and murder? And among them that he was 'awwab' i.e., returning to the remembrance of God, so how can it be conceived that he was persistent in intending the greatest sins? And among them that the mountains were subjected to him, glorifying with him in the evening and sunrise, and the birds were gathered, each turning to him; do you think that these things were subjected to him to use them as means to murder and adultery? And among them that he was given wisdom and decisive speech, and wisdom is a comprehensive term for everything that should be known and acted upon; so how can it be rational that he was characterized by wisdom while persisting in what the vilest devils would disdain—molesting his followers in their wives and concubines? And He praised him also after the story of the ewe by making him a vicegerent on earth, and this is among the greatest praises. And since the matter is so, why should the story be taken as referring to the famous story concerning David? Rather, a group planned to entrap him, and when they saw him alert, one of them invented the dispute mentioned in the Quran, and they claimed that they only approached him for its sake, not for any evil intent regarding killing.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage defends the infallibility of David (peace be upon him) by rejecting the well-known story involving Uriah's wife as a fabrication by the Hashwiyya (ignorant traditionists). The argument proceeds by listing the praises God gave David in the Quran before the 'story of the ewe' (the parable in 38:21-26): being 'possessor of hands' (power in religion), 'awwab' (returning to God), the subjugation of mountains and birds, and being given wisdom and decisive speech. The author argues that these lofty attributes are incompatible with the vile sins of adultery and murder attributed to him in the fabricated story. Furthermore, after the story of the ewe, God made David a vicegerent, which is a great praise. Therefore, the story cannot refer to that incident. Instead, the author suggests that a group plotted against David, and when their plot failed, they invented a dispute to explain their presence. Thus, the Quranic verses refer to that invented dispute, not to any sin of David.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story of David and Uriah's wife is historically true and accepted in Islam.
Correction: The story contradicts explicit Quranic praises of David and the doctrine of prophetic infallibility, thus it is a fabrication by the Hashwiyya.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the story of David and Uriah's wife fabricated?
A) It is not mentioned in the Quran
B) It contradicts the great praises God gave David in the Quran
C) The Bible says so
D) All prophets are infallible by consensus

Key Takeaway
The Quranic praises of David demonstrate his infallibility, proving the story of Uriah's wife is a fabrication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear stories that seem to diminish the status of prophets, do you critically evaluate them against the Quran's own praises of those prophets? What stories about David or other prophets do you need to re-examine?
?
If David is described as having power in religion — firm resolve to avoid sin — then what does your own power in religion look like? Are you truly committed to avoiding what God has forbidden, or do you make excuses for persistent sins?
?
The author questions how wisdom can coexist with persisting in sin. How does your own wisdom measure up? Are you living in a way that your actions match your knowledge of what is right, or is there a gap you need to close today?

The Correct Interpretation of the Trial of Prophet Dawud
Explains the trial of Dawud as a test of forbearance, not a sin, and refutes false interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of Prophet Dawud's trial as a test of forbearance, not a sin, and how it refutes false narrations.

Core Idea
Prophet Dawud's trial was a test of forbearance, not sin.

This lesson examines the story of Prophet Dawud (AS) mentioned in Surah Sad. The passage clarifies that the trial was about his forbearance when he suspected thieves, not about any sin. It refutes the false interpretation of the Hashwiyya and explains the meaning of forgiveness in the verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a rejection of attributing lying to angels.
2
Identify the key points about Dawud's trial
Find the explanation of the verse 'Indeed, We tried him' and what the test consisted of. Notice that Dawud's suspicion of the thieves and his forbearance in not punishing them is highlighted.
The test was about his forbearance, not about committing a sin.
3
Understand the refutation of false interpretations
The passage refutes the need to attribute lying to angels or reinterpret ewes as women. It also criticizes the Hashwiyya narrations. Identify these refutations.
Imam al-Razi affirms this as the clear truth.
4
Reflect on Imam al-Razi's affirmation
Read the quote from Imam al-Razi who declares that the story narrated by the Hashwiyya is false and invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النفس أو سرقة المال ) ونسبة الكذب إلى اللصوص أولى من نسبته إلى الملائكة)، وعلى هذا فمعنى قوله تعالى : إنما فتناه [يس : ٢٤] ، اختبرناه في أنه أساء حين الظن باللصوص مع قدرته عليهم فهل يعالجهم بالعقوبة أولا، فلما لم يعاقبهم كان غاية في الحلم والاستغفار لا يجب أن يكون لذنب منه بل جاز أن يكون طلباً لعفو وأن يغفر لهم مبالغة في الحلم والشفقة ، وقوله فغفرنا له [ ص : ٢٥ ] ، أي غفرنا لأجل حرمته وبركة شفاعته ذلك الفعل المنكر الذي أتى به أولئك المتسورون وحينئذ لا يحتاج إلى نسبة الكذب إلى الملائكة، وحمل النعاج على النسوان وخلط الذمة البليغة بأوصاف الكمال . قال الإمام الرازي: من أنصف علم أن الحق الصريح ما ذكرناه وأن تلك القصة كاذبة باطلة على الوجه الذي يرويها عليه أهل الحشوية، قوله : ( حين أساء الظن باللصوص إذا كان المظنون شراً أو ما يجري مجراه يطلق فيه إساءة الظن، وإن طابق الواقع كما يطلق حسن الظن في جنس الخير وإن لم يطابق المظنون الواقع ولذا قال أساء الظن .
and the soul or theft of money, and attributing lying to thieves is more appropriate than attributing it to angels. And upon this, the meaning of His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, We tried him' [Sad:24]: We tested him in that he thought ill of the thieves while having power over them, whether he would punish them or not. So when he did not punish them, it was extreme forbearance and seeking forgiveness, which does not have to be due to a sin from him, but rather it is possible that it was a request for pardon and that He forgive them, an exaggeration in forbearance and compassion. And His statement: 'So We forgave him that' [Sad:25] means: We forgave, due to his sanctity and the blessing of his intercession, that evil deed which those who had climbed over the wall did. And then there is no need to attribute lying to angels, or to interpret the ewes as women, or to mix the praiseworthy attributes with imperfections. Imam al-Razi said: Whoever is fair knows that the clear truth is what we mentioned, and that this story is false and invalid in the manner that the Hashwiyya narrate it. His statement: (When he thought ill of the thieves... if the object of thought is evil or something similar, it is called having a bad opinion, even if it matches reality, just as having a good opinion is called for general good even if it does not match reality. Therefore, it was said: he thought ill.)
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage clarifies the correct interpretation of the trial of Prophet Dawud (AS) mentioned in Surah Sad. It begins by stating that it is more appropriate to attribute lying to thieves than to angels, referring to the false interpretation that the two litigants were angels. Then it explains the verse 'Innama fatannahu' (Indeed, We tried him) as a test of Dawud's forbearance when he suspected the thieves; he had the power to punish them but did not, showing extreme forbearance. The forgiveness mentioned in the next verse ('So We forgave him that') is interpreted as forgiveness for the trespassers due to Dawud's intercession, not for a sin of Dawud. The passage rejects the need to reinterpret the ewes as women or to mix praiseworthy attributes with imperfections, as the Hashwiyya did. Imam al-Razi affirms this as the clear truth. The annotation at the end explains that 'having a bad opinion' (asaa' al-dhann) is a phrase used even if the suspicion matches reality, similar to 'having a good opinion' for good things.

إساءة الظن
الفتنة
الحشوية
النعاج
النسوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet Dawud committed a sin by judging incorrectly or by coveting another man's wife.
Correction: The trial was about his forbearance towards thieves, not about any sin. His forgiveness was for the trespassers, not for himself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the nature of the trial of Prophet Dawud (AS)?
A) He committed a sin by judging incorrectly.
B) He was tested in his forbearance when he thought ill of thieves and chose not to punish them.
C) He was tested by being tempted with a woman.
D) He was tested by loss of his kingdom.

Key Takeaway
The trial of Prophet Dawud was a test of forbearance, and the forgiveness mentioned refers to the forgiveness of the trespassers through his intercession, not any sin of his.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you suspect someone of wrongdoing, do you immediately confront them with harshness or do you pause and seek Allah's guidance to respond with forbearance like Dawud? What would change if you treated every suspicion as a test of your own character rather than a confirmation of their guilt?
?
This lesson shows that even prophets are tested with situations that could be misinterpreted as sin. Have you ever rushed to judge a fellow Muslim's action based on a surface-level understanding, only to later realize your mistake? How can you train yourself to give the best interpretation (husn al-ẓann) even when the evidence seems against it?
?
The forgiveness mentioned in the story is for the trespassers through Dawud's intercession, not for a sin of his. Reflect on your own intercessions: Do you seek forgiveness for others who have wronged you, or do you hold grudges? Are you willing to pray for those who have mistreated you, as Dawud apparently did?

Refuting the Objection About Prophet Sulayman's Prayer
Refutes the objection about missing prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars refute the claim that Prophet Sulayman (AS) missed the afternoon prayer due to his love of horses, and why forgetfulness is not considered a sin.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness negates sin; love for good things can be virtuous.

This lesson addresses a common objection against the sinlessness of prophets: the story of Prophet Sulayman allegedly missing a prayer because he was absorbed in admiring horses. We will examine the verse and the scholarly response, which clarifies that even if forgetfulness occurred, it is not a sin, and that the verse actually praises his love for good things as a means of remembering God's command.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from the verse
The objection is based on the verse 'When there were displayed before him in the afternoon the excellent horses.' It is claimed that this shows Sulayman missed a prayer due to distraction.
The verse is from Surah Sad, verse 31.
2
Understand the refutation: no indication of missing prayer
The first answer is that the verse does not indicate that the prayer was missed. It only mentions horses being displayed, not that he neglected his duty.
3
Note that forgetfulness is not a sin
Even if the prayer was missed due to forgetfulness, it would not be a sin. Prophets are protected from intentional sins, but forgetfulness does not violate their sinlessness.
This is an important principle in Islamic theology.
4
Interpret the love of good things and remembrance
Sulayman's statement 'I loved the love of good things' is an exaggeration of love, and it is for the sake of God's remembrance. The phrase 'from the remembrance of my Lord' means 'because of His command.'
The love is virtuous, not blameworthy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه قصة سليمان ( عليه السلام والتمسك بها ( من وجهين ) بل من وجوه : ( الأول : ( التمسك بقوله تعالى : ( إذ عرض عليه بالعشي ﴾ [ ص: ٣١] ) أي بعد الزوال ( الصافنات الجياد ﴾ [ ص: ۳۱] (الآية فإن ظاهره يدل على أن اشتغاله بتلك الصافنات الهاه عن ذكر الله حتى روي أنه فاتت عنه صلاة العصر ( والجواب أن لا دلالة فيه على فوت الصلاة مع أنه إذا كان فوتها بالنسيان لم يكن ذنباً، وقوله : و أحببت حب الخير [ ص: ۳۲] ، مبالغة في الحب) فإن الإنسان قد يحب شيئاً ولكن لا يجب أن يحبه فإذا أحبه وأحب أن يحبه فذلك هو الكمال في المحبة، (و) قوله ( عن ذكر ربي أي بسببه كما يقال : سقاه عن العيمة أي لأجلها فالمعنى أن ذلك الحب الشديد إنما حصل بسبب ذكره أي أمره ( لا) بالهوى ) وطلب الدنيا، وذلك لأن رباط الخيل في دينهم كان ( بأمره) كما في ديننا إذ هو مندوب إليه، . . (و) قوله (فطفق مسحاً معناه يمسح رؤوسها وأعناقها إكراماً لها وإظهاراً لشدة شفقته عليها لكونها من أعظم الأعوان في دفع أعداء الدين ( وحمله على قطعها)، كما ذهب إليه طائفة حيث قالوا : المعنى أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام جعل يمسح السيف بسوقها وأعناقها أي يقطعها إما غضباً عليها بسبب ما جرى عليه من أجلها وإما للتصدق بها، (ضعيف) جداً ( إذ لا دلالة للفظ عليه ) كما في قوله : وامسحوا برؤوسكم وأرجلكم [المائدة : ٦ ] ، نعم لو قيل : مسح السيف برأسه لربما فهم منه ضرب العنق وأما إذا لم يذكر السيف لم يفهم القطع البتة ( ورجوع ضمير تواترت إلى الشمس أبعد المحتملين) يريد أن ذلك الضمير يحتمل أن يعود إلى الشمس إذ قد جرى ما له تعلق بها وهي العشي وأن يعود إلى الصافنات وهذا أولى لأنها مذكورة صريحاً دون الشمس، وأيضاً هي أقرب في الذكر من لفظ العشي فالمعنى حينئذ أنه أمر بأعدائها حتى توارت بالحجاب أي غابت عن بصره ثم أمر بردها فما وصلت إليه أخذ يمسحها لما مر . قوله : (الصافنات الجياد الصافن من الخيل القائم على ثلاث قوائم وقد أقام الرابعة على طرف الحافر. قوله : ( سقاه عن العيمة) العيمة بالعين المهملة شهوة اللبن يقال : عام الرجل يعيم ويعام عيمة، والغيم بالغين المعجمة العطش والخوف وهذا وإن كان أنسب بالسياق إلا أن الغيمة بالتاء لم يذكر في الصحاح وإنما المذكور الغيم بدونها . قوله : (أي أمره) حمل الذكر على الأمر لأن الأمر سبب الذكر. قوله: (لأن رباط الخيل الرباط المرابطة وهي ملازمة ثغور العدو ورباط الخيل مرابطتها ويقال : الرباط الخيل الخمس فما فوقها .
And from it is the story of Sulayman (peace be upon him), and adhering to it is from two aspects, rather from many aspects: The first: Adhering to His statement the Exalted: 'When there were displayed before him in the afternoon' [Sad:31] i.e. after noon, 'the excellent horses' [Sad:31] the verse. For its apparent meaning indicates that his occupation with those horses distracted him from the remembrance of God, to the extent that it is narrated that the afternoon prayer was missed by him. The answer: There is no indication in it of missing the prayer, and even if it was missed due to forgetfulness, it would not be a sin. And His statement: 'And I loved the love of good things' [Sad:32] is an exaggeration in love. For a person may love something but he does not necessarily love to love it; if he loves it and loves to love it, that is perfection in love. And His statement: 'From the remembrance of my Lord' i.e. because of it, as it is said: 'he gave him to drink on account of thirst' i.e. for its sake. So the meaning is that that intense love only occurred due to His remembrance, i.e. His command, not by desire and pursuit of the world. That is because tying horses in their religion was by His command, just as in our religion it is recommended. ... And His statement: 'So he began to pass [his hand] over [the horses'] legs and necks' [Sad:33] meaning he stroked their heads and necks as an honor for them and a display of intense compassion for them, because they are among the greatest aids in repelling the enemies of the religion. And interpreting it as cutting them, as a group went to, saying that the meaning is that he (peace and blessings be upon him) began to pass the sword over their legs and necks i.e. cut them, either out of anger at them due to what happened to him because of them, or for charity, is very weak, because there is no linguistic indication for it, as in His statement: 'And wipe your heads and your feet' [al-Ma'idah:6]. Yes, if it were said: 'he passed the sword over its head', perhaps cutting the neck would be understood, but if the sword is not mentioned, cutting is not understood at all. And the returning of the pronoun of 'tawarat' to the sun is the more remote of the two possibilities. He intends that the pronoun could refer to the sun, since what is related to it has been mentioned, i.e. the afternoon, and could refer to the horses, and this is more likely because they are mentioned explicitly without the sun, and also they are closer in the mention than the word 'afternoon'. So the meaning then is that he ordered them to be brought until they were veiled by the screen i.e. disappeared from his sight, then he ordered them to be returned, and when they reached him he began to stroke them for the reason mentioned. His statement: 'al-Safinat al-Jiyad': al-Safin among horses is the one standing on three legs and has placed the fourth on the tip of the hoof. His statement: 'He gave him to drink on account of al-'aymah': al-'aymah with the unpointed 'ayn is a craving for milk. It is said: 'the man 'ama ya'imu wa ya'amu 'aymah.' And al-ghaym with the pointed ghayn is thirst and fear. And this, although more appropriate to the context, but 'al-ghaymah' with a ta' is not mentioned in al-Sihah; rather al-ghaym without it is mentioned. His statement: 'i.e. His command': the interpretation of 'dhikr' as 'command' because the command is the cause of remembrance. His statement: 'Because rabit al-khayl': al-ribat is al-murabatah, which is staying at the frontiers of the enemy, and rabit al-khayl is keeping them tied; and it is said: al-ribat is five horses or more.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the objection that Prophet Sulayman (AS) missed the afternoon prayer due to his absorption in the horses. It begins by stating the objection: the verse 'When there were displayed before him in the afternoon the excellent horses' is taken to imply that he was distracted from the remembrance of God, causing him to miss the prayer. The refutation first denies that the verse indicates missing the prayer, and even if it was missed through forgetfulness, forgetfulness is not a sin. Then it explains the verse 'I loved the love of good things' as an exaggeration of love that is commendable, not blameworthy. The phrase 'from the remembrance of my Lord' is interpreted as 'because of His command,' not as a distraction. The passage also discusses the meaning of 'mas'h' (stroking) and refutes the interpretation that he cut the horses' legs, citing the lack of linguistic evidence. The pronoun reference in 'tawarat' is argued to refer to the horses, not the sun. Finally, lexical notes define 'al-safinat', 'al-'aymah', 'rabit al-khayl', etc.

Key Terms
الصافنات الجياد
النسيان
رباط الخيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet Sulayman missed a prayer due to love of horses.
Correction: The verse does not indicate missing a prayer, and forgetfulness is not a sin.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary refutation of the claim that Prophet Sulayman missed the afternoon prayer?
A) The verse explicitly denies missing the prayer.
B) The verse does not indicate missing the prayer, and forgetfulness is not a sin.
C) The prayer was not obligatory at that time.
D) The horses were not real horses.

Key Takeaway
Prophet Sulayman did not sin; his love for horses was obedience to God's command and forgetfulness is not blameworthy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accused a prophet or righteous person of sin based on a superficial reading of the Quran? How does this lesson call you to trust in the perfection of the prophets and seek deeper understanding?
?
When you feel a strong attachment to something in this world—like wealth, status, or even a hobby—do you pause to ask whether this love is for God's command or for your own desire? What would it take to transform your love into an act of worship?
?
Think of a time you were consumed by guilt over a mistake you made unintentionally. How does the principle that forgetfulness is not a sin change the way you view God's mercy and your own imperfections? Will you let go of that guilt now?

The Meaning of 'Mas'h' and Pronoun Reference
Explains the true meaning of 'mas'h' and the pronoun reference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the interpretation of Prophet Sulayman cutting the horses' legs is weak and how the pronoun in 'tawarat' correctly refers to the horses.

Core Idea
Linguistic precision refutes false interpretations; context determines pronoun reference.

This lesson examines two additional aspects of the Sulayman story: the meaning of 'mas'h' (stroking) and the reference of the pronoun in 'tawarat' (disappeared). Scholars argue that 'mas'h' means stroking as an act of honor, not cutting, and that the horses are the subject, not the sun. We will explore the linguistic evidence for these interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the two interpretations of 'mas'h'
The passage presents two possibilities: stroking the horses' heads and necks as honor, or cutting their legs with a sword. The first is the correct interpretation.
The word 'mas'h' is used in the Quran for wiping, as in ablution.
2
Examine the linguistic argument against cutting
Cutting would require mentioning the sword. Without it, the word 'mas'h' alone does not imply cutting. The passage compares it to 'wipe your heads and feet' where no cutting is meant.
The weak interpretation is rejected due to lack of linguistic evidence.
3
Analyze the pronoun reference in 'tawarat'
The pronoun in 'tawarat' (disappeared) could refer to the sun or the horses. The passage argues it refers to the horses because they are explicitly mentioned and closer in the text.
This clarifies that the horses were brought until they disappeared from sight.
4
Review lexical notes
Definitions are given for 'al-safinat' (a horse standing on three legs), 'al-'aymah' (craving for milk), and 'rabit al-khayl' (tying horses for jihad).
These notes help in accurate interpretation of the verses.

?

The True Trial of Prophet Sulayman
Refutes the false story about Sulayman and explains true trial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the trial of Prophet Sulayman (AS) was not due to idol worship but due to omitting 'in sha Allah', as authentically interpreted by the Prophet (SAW).

Core Idea
Sulayman's trial was omission of 'in sha Allah', not idolatry.

This passage refutes a spurious story that claims Prophet Sulayman (AS) worshipped an idol. The authentic interpretation, directly from the Prophet (SAW), clarifies that the trial mentioned in Surah Sad was simply because he forgot to say 'in sha Allah'. Understanding this distinction protects the integrity of the prophets and highlights the importance of that phrase.

Guided Path
1
Read the false story first
The passage begins by presenting the false story that the Hashwiyya narrate. Read it carefully to see what is being refuted.
Notice the elements: statue, ring, repentance on ashes.
2
Then read the refutation
The answer refutes the story by citing the Prophet's hadith. The trial was for omitting 'in sha Allah', not for any sin of idolatry.
Pay attention to the hadith: Sulayman's oath about one hundred women.
3
Consider alternative interpretations
The passage also mentions other interpretations: illness or threat to his son. These serve to further distance the trial from any major sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ( التمسك بقوله تعالى : ( ولقد فتنا سليمان ) [ ص : ٣٤] وقصته أنه بلغ سليمان خبر ملك تحصن في جزيرة فخرج إليه بالريح وقتله وأخذ بنته وكانت في غاية الجمال ،فأحبها وكانت لا يرقأ لها دمع حزنا على أبيها فأمر سليمان الجن بأن يعملوا لها تمثالاً على صورة أبيها فكسته كسوة نفيسة وكانت تغدو وتروح إليها مع ولائدها يسجدن له على عادتهن في ملكه فسقط الخاتم من يد سليمان عليه السلام لعصيانه باتخاذ الصنم الذي يسجد له في بيته، فقال له آصف : إنك مفتون بذنبك فتب إلى الله فخرج إلى فلاة وقعد على الرماد تائباً إلى الله سبحانه وتعالى (الجواب : ( إن هذه الحكاية الخبيثة التي يرويها الحشوية كتاب الله مبرأ عنها ، فإنه قال ( النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام في تفسير هذا الكلام ( قال سليمان : أطوف الليلة على مئة امرأة تلد كل امرأة) منهن (ولداً يقاتل في سبييل (الله ولم يقل إن شاء الله (فلم تحمل من تلك المائة إلا واحدة فولدت نصف غلام فجاءت به القابلة فألقته على كرسيه بين يديه ولو أنه قال إن شاء الله كان كما قال، فالابتلاء المذكور في الآية ( إنما كان لترك الاستثناء ) لا لمعصية ( وقيل : ) ابتلاؤه كان بالمرض فإنه ) مرض حتى صار مشرفاً على الموت لا يقدر على حركة (كجسد بلا روح ، وقيل : ولد له ولد ) فقالت الشياطين: إن عاش ولده لم ينفك عن السحرة فعزمت على قتله فعلم سليمان ذلك فخاف الشياطين أن تهلكه فأمر، قوله : ( وقصته إلخ لا يرقا لها دمع أي لا يسكن من رقاً الدمع يرقا رقاء ورقؤ إذا سكن، والولائد جمع وليدة وهي الجارية والفلاة المفازة والجمع الفلا.
The second: Adhering to His statement the Exalted: 'And indeed We tried Sulayman' [Sad:34]. And the story: News reached Sulayman of a king who fortified himself on an island; so he went out against him by the wind, killed him, and took his daughter, who was extremely beautiful. He fell in love with her, and her tears would not dry from grief over her father. So Sulayman ordered the jinn to make a statue for her in the image of her father, and he clothed it with a precious garment. She would go to it morning and evening with her maidens, prostrating to it as was their custom in his kingdom. Then the ring fell from the hand of Sulayman (peace be upon him) due to his disobedience in taking the idol that was prostrated to in his house. So Asif said to him: You are tried because of your sin; repent to God. So he went out to a desert and sat on ashes, repenting to God Almighty. The answer: Indeed, this vile story that the Hashwiyya narrate, the Book of God is free from it. For he (the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him) said in the interpretation of this speech: Sulayman said: I will go around tonight to one hundred women; each woman will give birth to a child who will fight in the way of God. And he did not say 'if God wills'. So none of those hundred became pregnant except one, and she gave birth to half a child. The midwife brought it and placed it on his throne before him. If he had said 'if God wills', it would have been as he said. So the trial mentioned in the verse was only due to omitting the exception, not due to disobedience. And it is said: His trial was with illness, for he became ill until he was on the verge of death, unable to move, like a body without a soul. And it is said: A child was born to him, and the devils said: If his child lives, he will not be free from the sorcerers, so they resolved to kill him. Sulayman knew that, so he feared the devils would destroy him, so he ordered... His statement: (and his story etc. 'la yarqa' laha dam'): i.e., it does not settle. From 'raqa' al-dam' yarqa raqa'an wa ruqu'an if it settles. And al-wala'id is the plural of walidah, which is the slave girl, and al-falah is the desert, and the plural is al-fala.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a false story about Prophet Sulayman (AS) that the Hashwiyya narrate. The false story claims that Sulayman fell in love with a woman, made an idol for her, and lost his ring as punishment. The refutation cites the authentic interpretation from the Prophet (SAW): the trial was because Sulayman said he would visit one hundred women and have children, but omitted 'in sha Allah'. Consequently, only one woman became pregnant and gave birth to a half-child. The true trial was the omission of the exception, not a sin of idolatry. The passage also mentions alternative interpretations: that the trial was a severe illness, or that it involved the birth of a son who was threatened by devils, prompting Sulayman to issue orders. The annotation defines words: 'la yarqa' means it does not settle; 'wala'id' are slave girls; 'falah' is desert.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Prophet Sulayman worshipped an idol and lost his ring as punishment.
Correction: The Quranic trial was about omitting 'in sha Allah', not idolatry.

Checkpoint

B) He omitted saying 'in sha Allah' in his oath.
C) He was afflicted with a severe illness due to a sin.
D) He lost his ring and was overthrown by a demon.

Key Takeaway
The trial of Prophet Sulayman was a lesson in saying 'in sha Allah', not a sin of idolatry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The false story described a prophet repenting on ashes. Have you ever felt so ashamed of a sin that you isolated yourself in despair? What does this lesson teach you about true repentance that returns you to Allah's mercy rather than to self-punishment?

Answer to the Objection on Prophet Solomon's Request for a Kingdom
Refutes the claim that Solomon's request was sinful envy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars defend Prophet Solomon's request for a unique kingdom as a miracle rather than an act of envy.

Core Idea
Prophet Solomon's request was a miracle, not envy—tailored to his people's pride in kingship.

The objection that Solomon's request for a kingdom no one after him should have was an act of envy seems plausible at first. However, this passage from the commentary shows that the request was actually a divinely appointed miracle, consistent with the principle that prophets perform miracles in the field their people excel in. We will explore the objection, the answer, and a further objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage to find the objection: that Solomon's request was envy and thus a sin.
The objection is based on the Quranic verse where Solomon asks for a kingdom no one after him should have.
2
Understand the main answer
Study the answer which explains that a prophet's miracle is of the same type as what his people boast about. Solomon's people boasted of kingship, so his miracle was a unique kingdom.
The answer also clarifies that Solomon intended a non-transferable kingdom, symbolizing the kingdom of religion.
3
Examine the further question and its answer
Read the additional dialogue where a question is raised about the phrase 'after me' and understand the possible interpretations provided.
The phrase 'after me' is explained as 'other than me in my time' or as preventing future seizure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ملكا لا ينبغي بما حكى عنه في القرآن ( وهو قوله : هب لي لأحد ينبغي من بعدي ) فإنه حسد فيكون ذنباً ، الجواب : أنه ليس حسداً بل معجز كل نبي إنما كان من جنس ما يفتخر به أهل زمانه وكان ما افتخر به أهل زمان سليمان هو الملك أي المال والجاه فلا جرم طلب مملكة فائقة على جميع الممالك لتكون مملكته معجزة له وأراد أن ملك الدنيا موروث أي ينتقل من واحد إلى آخر فطلب من ربه بعدما شفي من مرضه الشديد ملك الدين الذي لا يمكن فيه الانتقال فقوله ملكاً لا ينبغي لأحد من بعدي أي ملكاً لا يمكن أن ينتقل عني إلى غيري وأراد الملك العظيم من القناعة وذلك أن الاحتراز عن لذات الدنيا مع القدرة عليها مما لا يمكن عادة فطلب الملك العظيم في الدنيا مع اشتغاله بطاعة ربه وعدم التفاته إلى ذلك الملك العظيم ليعلم الناس أن زخارف الدنيا لا تمنع من المولى، إن قلت : يكفي في الإعجاز حينئذ أن يعطي مملكة فائقة على مملكة أهل زمانه فلم قال لا ينبغي لأحد من بعدي . قلت : يحتمل أن يكون مراده من قوله من بعدي من غيري في زماني ويقرب منه قول القاضي أي لا ينبغي لأحد أن يسلبه مني بعد هذه السلبة .
The third [objection]: A kingdom that is not befitting what is related about him in the Qur'an (and it is his saying: Grant me one who is befitting after me) for it is envy, so it is a sin. The answer: It is not envy, but the miracle of every prophet is only of the same type as what the people of his time boast about. And what the people of Solomon's time boasted about was kingship (i.e., wealth and status). Hence he asked for a kingdom surpassing all kingdoms so that his kingdom would be a miracle for him. And he intended that the kingdom of this world is inherited (i.e., it passes from one to another), so he asked from his Lord after he was healed from his severe illness for the kingdom of religion, which cannot transfer. Thus his saying 'a kingdom not befitting anyone after me' means a kingdom that cannot transfer from me to another. And he intended the great kingdom of contentment, because avoiding the pleasures of this world while having power over them is not normally possible. So he asked for the great kingdom in this world while being occupied with obedience to his Lord and not paying attention to that great kingdom, so that people would know that the adornments of this world do not prevent from the Lord. If you said: Sufficient in the miracle then is that he be given a kingdom surpassing the kingdom of the people of his time, so why did he say 'not befitting anyone after me'? I said: It is possible that his intent in his saying 'after me' is 'other than me in my time', and close to it is the saying of al-Qadi: i.e., no one should take it away from me after this taking.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the infallibility of Prophet Solomon and its answer. The objection claims that Solomon's request for a kingdom that no one should have after him (Quran 38:35) was an act of envy, which is a sin incompatible with prophethood. The answer refutes this by explaining that the miracle of any prophet is of the same type as what his people boast of. Since Solomon's people boasted of kingship, his miracle was a kingdom surpassing all others. Moreover, he intended a kingdom that would not transfer to others, symbolizing the non-transferable kingdom of religion. The additional dialogue addresses a possible objection: if a surpassing kingdom were sufficient for a miracle, why specify 'after me'? The reply is that 'after me' may mean 'other than me in my time' or it may refer to preventing any future seizure, as understood by al-Qadi.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet Solomon's request for a unique kingdom was an act of envy, which is a sin.
Correction: The request was not envy but a divinely appointed miracle tailored to his people's pride in kingship, and the wording 'after me' clarifies its non-transferable nature.

Key Takeaway
Solomon's request for a unique kingdom was a miracle, not a sin, because it matched his people's pride in kingship and symbolized a non-transferable religious kingdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for something, do you examine your intention—is it for your own status or for a sign of His power? Solomon asked for a kingdom for a miracle. What would you ask for if you knew it would be a means for others to believe?
?
The passage says that avoiding worldly pleasures while having power over them is normally impossible. Are you content with your current state? Do you seek worldly gains while neglecting worship, or do you ask Allah to help you combine both?
?
Solomon tailored his miracle to his people's pride. Are you aware of the context of your own actions when calling others to Allah? Do you adapt your approach using what they value as a bridge, or do you insist on a one-size-fits-all method?

Defending Jonah's Infallibility: The Concept of Tark al-Awla
Defends Jonah's infallibility by interpreting his actions as leaving the better option.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of 'leaving the better option' (tark al-awla) is used to defend prophetic infallibility in the case of Prophet Jonah.

Core Idea
Jonah's 'sin' was leaving the better option, not actual sin.

This passage addresses a classic objection to prophetic infallibility: the apparent sins of Prophet Jonah. The objection lists his anger, his thought about God's restriction, and his confession of injustice. The answer reinterprets each point through the lens of 'leaving the better option' (tark al-awla), showing how Jonah's actions were either virtuous or permissible, not sinful. This lesson will walk through the objection and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection against Jonah's infallibility
Read the opening lines that list Jonah's anger, his thought, and his confession as apparent sins. Notice how strong terms like 'disbelief' and 'sin' are used.
This sets up the challenge to prophetic infallibility.
2
Understand the answer's reinterpretation of Jonah's anger
The answer argues that Jonah's anger was against stubborn disbelievers for the sake of God, making it righteous, not sinful.
Distinguish between sinful anger and praiseworthy zeal.
3
Understand the answer's reinterpretation of 'thought that We would not restrict him'
The phrase is reinterpreted from the root 'qadr' (restriction) meaning he expected ease, not a doubt in God's power.
Linguistic analysis resolves the apparent issue.
4
Understand the answer's reinterpretation of 'confessed injustice'
Jonah's confession is seen as self-reproach for leaving the better option, a form of humility and intensified supplication.
This preserves his infallibility while acknowledging a lesser shortcoming.
5
Reflect on the broader principle
The final warning 'be not like the companion of the fish' refers to patience in hardships, not to sin. Conclude that prophets can leave the better option but never commit sin.
This lesson clarifies a key aspect of prophetic infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه قصة يونس عليه السلام فإنه ذهب مغاضباً وظن أن لن يقدر الله عليه واعترف بكونه ظالماً والغضب ذنب والشك في قدرة الله تعالى كفر والظلم أيضاً ذنب والجواب : لعل غضبه كان على قوم كفرة بالغوا في العناد والمكابرة حتى عيل صبره ولم يطلق المصابرة معهم فهذا غضب لله على أعدائه فلا يكون ذنبا فظن أن لن نقدر عليه أن لن نضيق عليه فإنه مشتق من القدر كما في قوله : يبسط الرزق لمن يشاء ويقدر ﴾ [العنكبوت : ٦٢ ] ، لا من القدرة وإني الظالمين أي لنفسي بترك الأولى فاعترافه بالظلم هضم للنفس واستعظام لما صدر عنها مبالغة في التضرع ولا تكن كصاحب الحوت أي في قلة الصبر على الشدائد والمحن لتناول أفضل الرتب وليس معناه لا تكن مثله في ارتكاب الذنب.
And from it is the story of Jonah, peace be upon him: he went away angry and thought that We would not restrict him, and he confessed that he was an unjust person. Anger is a sin, doubt in the power of God is disbelief, and injustice is also a sin. The answer: Perhaps his anger was against a disbelieving people who exceeded in stubbornness and obstinacy until his patience ran out and he did not continue with them; this is anger for God against His enemies, so it is not a sin. 'And he thought that We would not restrict him' means that We would not tighten upon him, for it is derived from 'al-qadr' (restriction) as in His saying: 'He extends provision to whom He wills and restricts [it]' (Quran 29:62), not from 'al-qudra' (power). 'And I am among the unjust' means to myself by leaving the better option. So his confession of injustice is self-reproach and a magnifying of what emanated from him, an exaggeration in supplication. 'And be not like the companion of the fish' means in lack of patience in hardships and trials to attain the highest ranks; it does not mean do not be like him in committing sin.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage addresses objections regarding Prophet Jonah's infallibility. The objection lists his going away in anger, thinking God would not restrict him, and confessing injustice, all considered sins. The answer clarifies that Jonah's anger was against disbelievers for their obstinacy, making it a righteous anger for God, not a sin. The phrase 'thought that We would not restrict him' is interpreted as expecting ease, not a lack of divine power, based on the root 'qadr' (restriction). His confession 'I am among the unjust' is understood as leaving the better option (tark al-awla), a form of self-reproach to intensify supplication, not actual injustice. The warning 'be not like the companion of the fish' refers to patience in tribulations, not to sinning.

Key Terms
تارك الأولى
عصمة الأنبياء
يونس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophet Jonah sinned by his anger, doubt, and confession.
Correction: The passage shows these were not sins; anger was for God, thought was about restriction not power, and confession was self-reproach for leaving the better option.

Checkpoint
How is the phrase 'and he thought that We would not restrict him' interpreted in the answer?
A) As doubting God's power over him.
B) As a test of faith that he failed.
C) As a literal denial of God's ability.
D) As thinking God would not make things difficult for him, derived from 'qadr' (restriction) not 'qudra' (power).

Key Takeaway
Prophet Jonah's actions are not sins but examples of leaving the better option (tark al-awla), preserving his infallibility.

Defending the Prophet's Sinlessness Against the 'Erring' Objection
Answering the objection to prophetic sinlessness from Quran 93:7.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars reconcile the verse 'And He found you erring' (Quran 93:7) with the Prophet's sinlessness by interpreting it as pre-prophetic or worldly.

Core Idea
Prophetic 'erring' was never religious error, only pre-prophetic or worldly.

The Quranic verse 'He found you erring and guided you' might seem to contradict the Prophet's sinlessness. This lesson examines how scholars answer this objection by interpreting the 'erring' as either before prophethood or in worldly matters, consistent with other Quranic verses that explicitly negate religious error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then its literal English translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
Notice the two distinct answers given.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objection comes from Quran 93:7, where 'erring' is assumed to imply disobedience. Locate this verse in the passage.
3
Understand the two answers
The passage offers two interpretations: 'before prophethood' or 'worldly matters'. Note how each answer neutralizes the objection.
The first answer implies that before prophethood, the Prophet was not yet obligated to divine law.
4
Examine the supporting verse
Quran 53:2 is cited to confirm that in religious matters, the Prophet never strayed or erred. Understand why this forces one of the two interpretations.
5
Reflect on the reconciliation
Consider how the two verses are harmonized. The passage shows that apparent contradictions in the Quran can be resolved by proper contextualization.
This reinforces the doctrine of 'isma (sinlessness) of prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنه قصة نبينا والاحتجاج بها من وجوه الأول : ووجدك ضالا فهدى ﴾ [ الضحى : ٧]) ولا شك أن الضال عاص الجواب أنه قبل النبوة أو أراد ضالاً في أمور الدنيا ويجب حمله على هذا لقوله تعالى ما ضل صاحبكم وما غوى ﴾ [ النجم : ٢ ] إذ المراد به نفي الضلالة والغواية في أمور الدين بلا شبهة فوجه التوفيق بينهما ما ذكرنا.
And from it is the story of our Prophet, and the argumentation with it from several aspects. The first: 'And He found you erring and guided [you]' (Quran 93:7). There is no doubt that the one erring is disobedient. The answer: That was before prophethood, or he intended erring in worldly matters. And it must be taken in this sense because of His saying: 'Your companion has not strayed, nor has he erred' (Quran 53:2), for the intended meaning is the negation of straying and erring in religious matters without any doubt. So the way to reconcile the two is what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection based on Quran 93:7, where God says He found the Prophet 'erring' and guided him, implying that the Prophet was previously disobedient. The answer offers two interpretations: either this refers to the time before prophethood (when he was not yet sinless in religious terms) or it refers to being unaware of worldly matters (such as lost in the desert) rather than religious error. A supporting argument is given from Quran 53:2, which explicitly states that the Prophet has not strayed or erred, and this is understood to apply to religious matters. Thus, the 'erring' in 93:7 must be interpreted in a way consistent with the later verse, namely, as pre-prophetic or worldly. The passage thus defends Prophet Muhammad's sinlessness.

Key Terms
ضال
عصمة الأنبياء
قبل النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Quran 93:7 indicates the Prophet committed a sin or error in religious matters after becoming a prophet.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the 'erring' is either before prophethood or in worldly matters (e.g., physical disorientation), never a religious transgression.

Checkpoint
What is one explanation given for the verse 'And He found you erring and guided you'?
A) It refers to a major sin he committed before becoming a prophet.
B) It refers to him being lost in worldly affairs before prophethood.
C) It refers to a moment of forgetfulness during revelation.
D) It contradicts Surah al-Najm, so it is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'found you erring' does not contradict the Prophet's sinlessness because it refers to either pre-prophetic status or worldly matters, not religious error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used the fact that you were not yet guided as an excuse to commit a sin? How does the Prophet's protection even before prophethood challenge the comfort you take in your own past sins?
?
If the Prophet was kept from religious error, what does that demand of your trust in the sunnah when it conflicts with your desires? Are you ready to submit without reservation?
?
The objector saw one verse and jumped to a conclusion. Where in your life have you rejected a deep truth because of a quick, superficial reading? Will you commit to seeking full understanding before dismissing a doctrine?

Refuting the Gharaniq Incident: Satanic Interpolation
Presents the objection and the satanic interpolation answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first answer to the Gharaniq story preserves the Prophet's infallibility by attributing the offending words to Satanic interpolation.

Core Idea
The Prophet never uttered the praise of idols; Satan mixed his voice with the recitation.

The Gharaniq story is often used to challenge prophetic infallibility. In this passage, the theologian presents the objection and then offers the primary defense: the words were not recited by the Prophet but were thrown onto his tongue by Satan. This explanation maintains the Prophet's sinlessness while accounting for the reported incident.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection narrative
Read the full account of the Gharaniq incident as reported by critics. Note how the story describes the Prophet's distress and the alleged insertion of verses.
The narrative is presented as an objection to prophetic infallibility.
2
Identify the first answer
Focus on the key phrase 'من إلقاء الشيطان'. Understand that the answer claims Satan recited the phrase and mixed his voice with the Prophet's.
This preserves the Prophet from actually uttering falsehood.
3
Reflect on the necessity of this defense
Consider why the theologian emphasizes that if the Prophet had recited the words, it would be disbelief—impermissible by consensus.
The defense upholds the doctrine of 'isma (infallibility).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : ما روي أنه ( عليه الصلاة والسلام لما اشتد عليه إعراض قومه عن دينه تمنى أن يأتيه من الله ما يتقرب به إليهم ويستميل قلوبهم فأنزل الله عليهم سورة والنجم، فلما اشتغل بقراءتها ( قرأ بعد قوله تعالى : أفرأيتم اللات والعزى ﴾ [ النجم : ١٩]، ومناة الثالثة الأخرى تلك الغرانيق العلى منها الشفاعة ترتجى ( فلما سمعه قريش فرحوا به وقالوا : قد ذكر الهتنا بأحسن الذكر ( فأتاه جبريل ( عليه السلام بعدما أمسى ( وقال ) له ( تلوت على الناس ما لم أتله عليك ) ، فحزن النبي لذلك حزناً شديداً الله خوفاً عظيماً ( فنزل ) لتسليته ( وما أرسلنا من قبلك من رسول ولا نبي إلا إذا تمنى ألقى الشيطان في أمنيته إلخ والجواب على تقدير حمل التمني على القراءة هو ( أنه من إلقاء الشيطان ( يعني أن الشيطان قرأ هذه العبارة المنقولة وخلط صوته بصوت النبي ( حتى ظن أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام قرأها وإلا ) أي وإن يكن من إلقائه بل ( كان ) النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام قارئالها كان ( ذلك كفراً صادراً عنه وليس بجائز إجماعاً ) وأيضاً ربما كان ما ذكر من العبارات (قرآناً وتكون الإشارة بتلك الغرانيق إلى الملائكة فنسخ تلاوته للإيهام أي لإيهامه المشركين أن المراد به آلهتهم ) أو (المراد على تقدير حمل أتمنى شيئاً وسوس إليه الشيطان ودعاه إلى ما لا ينبغي ثم إن الله تعالى ينسخ ذلك ويهديه إلى ترك الالتفات إلى وسوسته، وعلى هذا تكون الرواية المذكورة من مفتريات الملاحدة (أو) نقول عن التقدير الأول أيضاً ( هو ) أي قوله تلك الغرانيق إلى آخره كان من القرآن وأريد بالغرانيق الأصنام لكنه ) استفهام إنكار) حذف منه أداته فالمعنى أن هذه المستحقرات ليست كما تدعونها وترجون الشفاعة منها .
The second [objection]: What is narrated that when the Prophet (peace be upon him) was distressed by his people's turning away from his religion, he wished that God would send him something by which he could draw near to them and win their hearts. Then God revealed to them Surat al-Najm. When he began reciting it, after His saying: 'Have you considered al-Lat and al-Uzza?' (Quran 53:19), and Manat, the third, the other? 'Those are the high gharaniq (cranes), from them intercession is hoped.' When Quraysh heard that, they rejoiced and said: 'He has mentioned our gods in the best way.' Then Gabriel came to him after evening and said: 'You have recited to the people what I did not bring to you.' So the Prophet became extremely sorrowful and feared greatly. Then was revealed to console him: 'And We did not send before you any messenger or prophet but when he desired, Satan threw into his desire' etc. The answer, on the assumption that 'desire' means recitation, is that it is from Satan's throwing (i.e., Satan recited that transmitted phrase and mixed his voice with the Prophet's voice) until it was thought that the Prophet had recited it. Otherwise (i.e., if the Prophet had actually recited it), that would be disbelief issuing from him, and it is not permissible by consensus. Also, perhaps those expressions were (originally) Quran, and the reference by 'gharaniq' was to angels, but its recitation was abrogated to give the illusion (to the polytheists that their gods were meant). Or, on the assumption that 'desire' was something else, Satan whispered to him and called him to something improper, then God abrogates that and guides him to disregard the whisper. On this basis, the mentioned narration is a fabrication of the atheists. Or, we say regarding the first interpretation as well: his saying 'those are the high gharaniq' to the end was from the Quran, and by 'gharaniq' were meant the idols, but it is a rhetorical question whose interrogative particle was deleted; so the meaning is that these despised things are not as you claim and you hope for intercession from them.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage deals with the contentious 'Gharaniq' story, an objection to the Prophet's infallibility. The objection states that the Prophet, wishing to win over Quraysh, recited verses praising their idols. The answer provides multiple defenses: First, that the words were not recited by the Prophet but thrown by Satan onto his tongue, so the Prophet did not actually utter them. If he had, it would be kufr, which is impermissible for prophets. Second, those words might have been genuine Quran referring to angels, later abrogated to test the polytheists. Third, the narration itself might be a fabrication by heretics. Fourth, the verse might be a rhetorical question rejecting the polytheists' claim. The passage thus comprehensively refutes the story to preserve the Prophet's sinlessness.

Key Terms
إلقاء الشيطان
الغرانيق
عصمة الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that the Prophet intentionally praised idols to win over Quraysh, which would violate his infallibility.
Correction: The Prophet did not utter those words; Satan interjected them without the Prophet's awareness, as stated in the answer.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, how did the offending words enter the recitation?
A) The Prophet recited them deliberately.
B) Gabriel commanded him to recite them.
C) Satan recited them and mixed his voice with the Prophet's.
D) The words were part of the original revelation but later abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The primary defense of the Gharaniq incident is that Satan interpolated the words, not the Prophet, thus maintaining prophetic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading this lesson, do you truly believe that the Prophet never faltered in conveying the message, even for a moment? If a part of you doubts, what does that doubt reveal about your trust in Allah's protection of the revelation?
?
The Prophet was deeply grieved when he thought he had recited something wrong. Have you ever been grieved by a mistake in your own worship or speech about Allah? How does that grief move you to be more careful with what you say about your faith?
?
If Satan could mix his voice with the Prophet's recitation without the Prophet knowing, how much more subtle are the deceptions in your own heart? What steps will you take today to guard your tongue and your recitation against the whisperings of the accursed?

Alternative Explanations for the Gharaniq Incident
Offers three alternative defenses for the Gharaniq incident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three additional responses to the Gharaniq story: abrogation, fabrication, and rhetorical question.

Core Idea
The Gharaniq verses were either genuine Quran abrogated, a heretic fabrication, or a rhetorical question.

Beyond the primary answer of satanic interpolation, scholars provided further defenses to address the Gharaniq story from every angle. These alternatives—abrogation, fabrication, and rhetorical question—each reinforce the doctrine of prophetic infallibility by showing how the story cannot be taken at face value.

Guided Path
1
Understand the abrogation explanation
Read the argument that the phrases might have been original Quran referring to angels, but their recitation was abrogated to test the polytheists.
This preserves the words as divine but later removed.
2
Examine the fabrication claim
Note the suggestion that the entire narration is a fabrication by atheists (ملاحدة). This dismisses the story entirely.
3
Analyze the rhetorical question interpretation
See how the phrase can be read as a rhetorical question denying the polytheists' claims, with the interrogative particle omitted.
This turns the apparent praise into a refutation.

?

Answer to the Objection of Zayd's Marriage
Defends the Prophet's marriage to Zaynab as a divine command to abrogate custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Prophet's marriage to Zaynab was a divine command to abrogate the pre-Islamic prohibition of marrying the wives of adopted sons.

Core Idea
The Prophet married Zaynab by God's command, not desire.

This lesson addresses a common objection against the Prophet's marriage to Zaynab. Critics claimed he acted out of personal desire, but the divine answer reveals a deeper purpose: to abrogate a pre-Islamic custom. Let's examine the passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's words sink in.
Notice the flow from objection to answer.
2
Identify the objection and answer
The passage begins with 'The third [objection]' and then gives the answer. Find the core claim of the objection and how the answer refutes it.
The answer uses 'بل كان بأمر الله' implicitly.
3
Understand abrogation (نسخ)
The key term نسخ refers to the abrogation of a previous ruling. Here, God abrogates the pre-Islamic prohibition of marrying the wives of adopted sons.
This explains why the marriage was permissible and even commanded.
4
Reflect on the Prophet's concealment
The verse 33:37 mentions the Prophet's initial hesitation. The passage explains it was out of fear of hypocrites' criticism, not sin.
This preserves the Prophet's infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : قصة زيد وزينب والجواب : أنه أي نکاح زينب كان بأمر الله تعالى لنسخ ما كان في الجاهلية من تحريم أزواج الأدعياء وإنما أخفى في نفسه ذلك خوفاً من طعن المنافقين، وتوضيحه أن الله تعالى لما أراد أن ينسخ ذلك التحريم أوحى إليه أن زيداً إذا طلق زوجته فتزوج بها.
The third [objection]: The story of Zayd and Zaynab. The answer: That (i.e., the marriage to Zaynab) was by the command of God Almighty to abrogate what was in the pre-Islamic period of the prohibition of the wives of adopted sons. He only concealed that in himself out of fear of the reproach of the hypocrites. Its clarification is that when God intended to abrogate that prohibition, He revealed to him that when Zayd divorces his wife, he should marry her.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection concerning the Prophet's marriage to Zaynab, the divorced wife of his adopted son Zayd. The objection claims that the Prophet acted out of personal desire. The answer states that this marriage was commanded by God to abrogate the pre-Islamic custom that considered the wife of an adopted son like a biological daughter-in-law, forever forbidden. The Prophet's initial concealment of his intention was out of concern for the hypocrites' criticism, not sin. The explanation continues that God revealed the command in advance, instructing the Prophet to marry Zaynab after Zayd divorced her, thus establishing the new ruling.

Key Terms
نسخ
أزواج الأدعياء
عصمة الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet married Zaynab out of personal desire.
Correction: The marriage was commanded by God to abrogate the pre‑Islamic prohibition of marrying the wives of adopted sons.

Checkpoint
Why did the Prophet marry Zaynab according to the answer?
A) Out of love for Zaynab despite societal norms.
B) To follow the pre-Islamic custom of adopted sons' wives.
C) By God's command to abrogate the pre-Islamic prohibition.
D) To strengthen ties with Zayd's family.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's marriage to Zaynab was a divine command to abrogate a pre‑Islamic custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Prophet concealed his intention not out of sin, but to shield the truth from those who would distort it. Is there an area of your life where you have concealed your Islamic commitment out of fear? How can you align your inner conviction with your outer actions starting today?
?
This passage shows that God abrogates customs that oppose His law. What deeply ingrained habit or social custom in your life contradicts God's command? Will you ask God for the strength to break it and follow His guidance, even if it feels difficult?
